There is no danger,‚Äù she continued, sternly, as Miss Sallie began to protest at the idea of one of her girls going off with all those strange men. ‚ÄúA Gypsy does not repay a kindness with a blow. Come,‚Äù she called to the men, ‚Äúthat young lady will show the way.‚Äù And she pointed at Barbara, who had slipped the pistol into her belt, and was talking to Ruth in a low voice.

Miss Sallie explained to the girls what Granny Ann had decided was the best course for them to take, while the four men untethered the four lean horses and half-harnessed them, and the old Gypsy man gathered some coils of rope together.

Ruth insisted on accompanying Barbara, and the two girls led the way through the wood to the road, the men following with the horses. 73

They found the automobile exactly as it had been left, save in one particular. The murderous-looking dagger was gone. But the suit cases and numerous dress boxes were untouched.

The girls waited at one side while the Gypsies secured the ropes to the car and then to the collars of the horses. Two Gypsies walked on either side, holding the reins, while the other two ran to the back and began to push the machine. The horses strained at the ropes; then in an instant the automobile was moving easily, urged from the back and pulled from the front like a stubborn mule.

When the girls again reached that part of the road opposite the camp, the caravan came to a full stop.

Ruth directed that all the cushions be carried to the tent, together with the steamer rugs stored under the seats, the tea-basket and other luggage. The dismantled automobile was then left for the night.

Ruth and Bab found Miss Sallie waiting at the tent, a tragic figure in the darkness.

74CHAPTER VII‚ÄîA NIGHT WITH THE GYPSIES

‚ÄúI think we shall be comfortable enough, Aunt Sallie,‚Äù said her niece, after their belongings had been deposited in the tent. ‚ÄúWe will fix you a nice bed, auntie, dearest, with steamer rugs and your rubber air cushion, and for the first time in your life you will be almost sleeping under the stars.‚Äù

But poor Miss Sallie only smiled in reply. She was too weary and exhausted to trust the sound of her own voice, now that danger was over and they had found protectors.

While Grace and Ruth arranged three beds inside the tent (Ruth and Bab having joyfully elected to sleep just outside) the two sisters made tea and opened up boxes of tea biscuits and Swiss chocolate which were always kept in the provision basket for emergencies.

Granny Ann had offered them food, but they had courteously declined, remembering tales they had heard of the unclean Gypsy, and giving as an excuse that they had a light supper with them. ‚ÄúVery light indeed,‚Äù commented Ruth later; ‚Äúbut I don‚Äôt think we‚Äôll starve.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow that everything is comfy,‚Äù observed 75 Grace, ‚ÄúI, for one, think it is great fun. Our little house in the woods! For one night, it is almost as good as the cabin in the Berkshires.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, for one night; but give me a roof when the rain comes,‚Äù cried Ruth.

‚ÄúYou are safe for to-night, at any rate, Ruth,‚Äù said Barbara, looking up at the sky through the branches of the tall forest trees. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs not a cloud, even as small as a man‚Äôs hand. And how bright the stars are! There comes the harvest moon. It looks like a great, red lantern.‚Äù

‚ÄúMoney, money!‚Äù cried Mollie excitedly.

‚ÄúWhat is the matter with you, child?‚Äù said Miss Sallie, startled into finding her voice at last.

‚ÄúDidn‚Äôt you see it?‚Äù said Mollie. ‚ÄúIt was a splendid shooting star. It had a tail that reached halfway across the heavens. Don‚Äôt you know that, if you remember to say ‚Äòmoney, money, money,‚Äô before it fades out of sight or goes wherever it disappears to‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

‚Äú‚ÄòOh, mother, where do the shooting stars go‚Äô?‚Äù laughed Ruth, breaking in upon Mollie‚Äî‚Äúyou will inherit a large sum of money,‚Äù continued Mollie.

‚ÄúWe shall be sleeping at the feet of an heiress, then,‚Äù said Bab. ‚ÄúOr did the star fade out before you had finished, Molliekins?‚Äù 76

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know,‚Äù replied Mollie. ‚ÄúI was so excited that I forgot to look.‚Äù

By this time tea was ready and a rug had been spread in front of the tent for the guests to sit upon. Miss Sallie with her air cushion between her shoulders and the trunk of a tree that spread its branches over the tent, was beginning to feel that life, after all, held a number of pleasant things, including a certain favorite blend of tea that was as delicious, fragrant and expensive as heart could wish.

The night breeze touched their faces gently, and the stillness and sweet scents of the woods soothed them into forgetfulness of their troubles. While they sipped their tea and talked, in subdued voices, of the mystery of the forest at night, the Gypsy girl crept up and gazed curiously, almost wistfully, at them.

‚ÄúDo have some chocolate,‚Äù called Ruth, as she held the box toward the girl. ‚ÄúCome over and sit down, won‚Äôt you? What is your name?‚Äù

‚ÄúMy name is Zerlina,‚Äù replied the Gypsy, as she nibbled gingerly at a piece of chocolate.

‚ÄúAnd is Granny Ann your mother?‚Äù asked Ruth.

‚ÄúShe is my grandmother,‚Äù replied Zerlina. ‚ÄúMy mother died many years ago.‚Äù

Ruth looked at her sympathetically. They 77 had, she thought, at least one thing in common in their widely separated circumstances.

‚ÄúWould you like,‚Äù she asked gently, ‚Äúto live in a city and go to school?‚Äù

For a moment Zerlina‚Äôs face flushed with a deep glow of color. Her eyes traveled from one to another of the automobile party. She noted their refined, well-bred faces, their dainty dresses, the luxurious pile of long silk coats and chiffon veils. Nothing escaped the child, not even the elegant little tea basket with its fittings of silver and French china.

‚ÄúThere are times when I hate this life,‚Äù Zerlina said finally, turning to Ruth, who was watching her curiously. ‚ÄúThere are times in the winter when we have been too poor to go far enough South to keep warm. It is then that I would like the city and the warm houses. But my grandmother is very strict.‚Äù

She paused and bit her lip. She had spoken so fiercely that the girls had felt somewhat embarrassed at their own prosperity. ‚ÄúBut,‚Äù continued Zerlina in a quieter tone, ‚Äúwhen summer comes, I would rather be here in the woods. Gypsies do not live in houses,‚Äù she went on a little proudly. ‚ÄúMy grandmother has told me that they have been wanderers for thousands of years. They do not go to school. They teach each other. My grandmother has taught me to 78 read and write. She was taught by her mother, who was adopted and educated by a noble lady. But she came back to the Gypsies afterwards.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd your mother?‚Äù asked Mollie.

‚ÄúMy mother is dead,‚Äù returned Zerlina, and closed her lips tightly, as if to block all further inquiries in that direction.

‚ÄúIt is very interesting!‚Äù exclaimed Ruth. ‚ÄúAnd your education is then really inherited from your great-grandmother.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù assented the girl, ‚Äúbut I have inherited more than that‚Äîfrom my mother.‚Äù

The girls waited for Zerlina to finish. They hesitated to question her about her mother since it was evidently a forbidden subject with her.

‚ÄúI have inherited her voice,‚Äù she added confidentially. ‚ÄúIt may be that I shall be a singer some day.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, really?‚Äù cried all the girls in unison.

‚ÄúYou will sing for us now, won‚Äôt you?‚Äù added Ruth.

‚ÄúIf you wish,‚Äù said Zerlina. ‚ÄúI will get my guitar.‚Äù And she disappeared in the darkness.

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt she pretty?‚Äù commented Mollie.

‚ÄúHow soft her voice is, and what good English she speaks,‚Äù marveled Ruth. ‚ÄúBut then, we must remember her great-grandmother was 79 educated by a noble lady and transmitted her learning and manners straight to her.‚Äù

‚ÄúPoor thing!‚Äù exclaimed Bab. ‚ÄúI am really very sorry for her. The instincts of her great-grandmother and her grandmother keep up a sort of warring inside of her. ‚ÄúBut it‚Äôs mostly the open she gets,‚Äù said Grace. ‚ÄúWhat do you suppose she meant when she said that Granny Ann was very strict?‚Äù

‚ÄúI can‚Äôt imagine,‚Äù replied Ruth, ‚Äúunless Granny Ann refuses to allow her to buy herself a warm house. Seriously, though, I should like to do something for a girl like Zerlina. Zerlina emerged from the darkness, with an old guitar, and, sitting crosslegged on the ground, began to thrum an accompaniment. Then she sang in a deep, rich voice a song of the Gypsies. The song was in Spanish and the beat of the music was so weird and insistent that the listeners could hardly restrain themselves from joining hands and dancing in time to the rhythm.

They were thrilled by the romance of the 80 Gypsy camp and the charm of the girl‚Äôs singing. When she had finished they begged for more, and Zerlina was about to comply when a voice called her from the encampment. It was her grandmother‚Äôs, and what she said was not understood, since it was in the Romany language. But the girl leaped hurriedly to her feet.

‚ÄúI will not sing again to-night,‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúThe ladies are tired. Another time. Good-night,‚Äù And she slipped away in the darkness.

‚ÄúGranny Ann is strict,‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúYou wouldn‚Äôt think she would object to Zerlina‚Äôs associating with a few girls her own age. I wonder why she doesn‚Äôt like to have her sing? Perhaps she is afraid she will run away, some day, and go on the stage.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wish I had her beautiful voice,‚Äù sighed Grace. ‚ÄúThink what it could be made with proper training.‚Äù

‚ÄúIf she does not coarsen in feature, as so many of these dark women do,‚Äù observed Miss Sallie, ‚Äúshe will be very handsome some day.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd now for our lowly beds,‚Äù cried Ruth. ‚ÄúBarbara, you and I will sleep at the door of the tent like faithful slaves guarding their noble ladies. Nobody need be afraid. ‚ÄúMollie, you seem to be on such friendly terms with these people. What is your opinion?‚Äù asked Miss Sallie.

‚ÄúI believe we shall be as safe as if we were in our own homes,‚Äù replied Mollie. ‚ÄúGranny Ann will keep faith with us. You will see. But I know she‚Äôs a kind of queen in the tribe, and used to being obeyed.‚Äù

Fifteen minutes had hardly slipped past when Miss Sallie and ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls‚Äù were sound asleep, Bab with her pistol at her side.

CHAPTER VIII‚ÄîTHE HAUNTED POOL

To be awakened early in the morning by the songs of birds and innumerable woodland sounds, and find one‚Äôs self in the very center of a forest, is no common experience. To the girls, as they looked up through the leafy canopies, and then across the green aisles formed by trees that looked as if they might have stood there since the beginning of time‚Äîit was all very wonderful. 82

‚ÄúHow beautiful this is!‚Äù exclaimed each one, as she opened her eyes upon the wooded scene.

‚ÄúGirls,‚Äù cried Ruth, ‚ÄúI wouldn‚Äôt have missed this for worlds! No wonder Zerlina hates to live in a house in the summer time. Isn‚Äôt this fun? Shall we go over there and wash our faces in that little brook!‚Äù

Off they scampered, a curious procession for the deep woods, each with a burden of toilet articles, soaps and sponges, wash rags, mirrors and brushes.

‚ÄúWell,‚Äù exclaimed Miss Sallie Stuart as she knelt beside the stream and dipped her hands into its cool depths, ‚ÄúI never expected to come to this; but it is very refreshing, nevertheless.‚Äù

‚ÄúThis is Nature‚Äôs bathtub, auntie, dear. We should be thankful to have it so near. I suppose that is the reason the Gypsies chose this spot to camp in,‚Äù said Ruth.

‚ÄúMy dear child,‚Äù replied her aunt, ‚ÄúI know very little about the Gypsy race; but I do know one thing: that a Gypsy never took advantage of any kind of a bathtub, wooden, tin, porcelain or Nature‚Äôs.‚Äù

The girls all laughed joyously.

The fright of the day before had not left a very deep impression. Sleep and a feeling of safety had almost effaced it.

Presently they were back at the tent making 83 tea and boiling eggs supplied by Granny Ann from the Gypsy larder. Ruth wanted to build a fire, but they decided that the ground was too dry to risk it. The Gypsies had dug a small trench all around their camp fire. If they had not, those splendid old woods would have been in serious danger of burning, explained Barbara, who had been reading a great deal in the papers about forest fires.

It was arranged, after breakfast, that one of the men should ride over with a note to Major Ten Eyck‚Äôs, asking the major to send for them at once, and also to dispatch his chauffeur to mend the slashed tires.

The Gypsy camp had been astir long before the automobilists arose, and the men were now sitting at their ease around the clearing, smoking silently, while Granny Ann and two other women were moving about the tents, ‚Äúcleaning up,‚Äù as Ruth expressed it.

‚ÄúThey have a lovely chance to learn housework,‚Äù said Grace. ‚ÄúBut they do seem to air their bedclothes. Look at all those red comforts hanging on the bushes.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs easier to air them than to make up the beds,‚Äù observed Mollie. ‚ÄúAll you have to do in the morning, is to hang your blanket on a hickory limb, and when you go to bed, snatch it off the limb and wrap up in it for the night.‚Äù 84

‚ÄúDo you suppose they sleep in their clothes?‚Äù pondered Barbara.

‚ÄúWhy, of course they do,‚Äù replied Ruth. ‚ÄúYou don‚Äôt for a moment imagine they would ever go to the trouble of undressing, only to dress again in the morning?‚Äù

‚ÄúGirls, girls,‚Äù remonstrated Miss Sallie, ‚Äúwe must not forget that we are accepting their hospitality. Besides, here comes that young woman with the voice.‚Äù

‚ÄúLet‚Äôs take Zerlina as a guide, and go for a walk,‚Äù cried Ruth. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm so full of life and spirits this morning that I couldn‚Äôt possibly sit down like those lazy men over there, who seem to have nothing to do but smoke and talk. ‚ÄúI certainly have no idea of going for a walk,‚Äù replied Miss Stuart, ‚Äúafter what I‚Äôve been through with these last two days. Nor do I want you to go far, either, or I shall be terribly uneasy.‚Äù

But Miss Sallie was not really uneasy. It was one of those enchanting mornings when the mind is not troubled with unpleasant feelings. Perhaps the Gypsies had bewitched her. At any rate she sat back comfortably among the cushions and rugs, with her writing tablet, the 85 new magazines and the latest novel all close at hand, and watched the girls until they disappeared down the leafy aisles of the forest. How charming their voices sounded in the distance! How sweet was the sound of their young laughter! Miss Stuart closed her eyes contentedly. The spell of the place was upon her, and she fell asleep before she had opened a single magazine or cut one leaf of the new novel.

In the meantime, the four girls, led by Zerlina and her dog, were following the little stream in its capricious windings through the forest.

A squirrel darted in front of them with a flash of gray and jumped to the limb of a tree.

Zerlina made a sign for the girls to be silent. Then speaking to her dog in her own language, he sat down immediately on his haunches and never moved a muscle until she spoke to him again. She walked slowly toward the tree, where the squirrel sat watching them uneasily. A few feet off she paused and gave a shrill, peculiar whistle. The squirrel pricked up his ears and cocked his head on one side. Zerlina whistled again and held out her hand. The charm was complete. Down the limb he crept until he reached the ground, paused again, surveyed the scene with his little black eyes, and with one leap, settled himself on her shoulder. 86

‚ÄúOh!‚Äù cried the impulsive Ruth and the spell was broken.

Away scampered the frightened little animal.

‚ÄúHow wonderful!‚Äù exclaimed the others as they gathered around Zerlina, who held herself with a sort of proud reserve as they plied her with questions.

‚ÄúIt is because I have lived in the woods so much of the time,‚Äù she explained. ‚ÄúOne makes friends with animals when one has no other friends.‚Äù

‚ÄúZerlina,‚Äù said Ruth, ‚Äúlet me be your friend.‚Äù

‚ÄúThank you,‚Äù replied the girl simply, ‚Äúbut perhaps we shall not meet again. You will be going away in a little while.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou must come and sing for us at Major Ten Eyck‚Äôs,‚Äù said Ruth, ‚Äúand then we shall see if we cannot meet again.‚Äù

They were walking in single file, now, along the stream. Mollie was gathering ferns which grew in profusion on the bank. Barbara, who was behind the others, had stopped to look at a bird‚Äôs nest that had fallen to the ground and shattered the little blue eggs it had held.

As she knelt on the ground, something impelled her to look over her shoulder. At first Bab saw only the green depths of the forest, but in a moment her eyes had found what had attracted them. 87 Stifling a cry she rose to her feet. What she had seen was gone in an instant, so quickly that she wondered if she had not been dreaming. Peering at her through the leaves of parted branches she had seen a face, a very strange, old face, as white as death. It was the face of an old person, she felt instinctively, but the eyes had something childlike in their expression of wonder and surprise.

When it was gone, Barbara felt almost as if she had seen a ghost. She leaned over and dipped her hands into the stream to quiet her throbbing veins.

‚ÄúTruly this wood is full of mysteries,‚Äù she thought to herself as she turned to follow the others. But she decided not to say anything about it. They had had enough frights lately, and she was determined not to add another to the list.

By this time the girls had reached a lovely little pool set like a mirror in a mossy frame. On one side the bank had flattened out and was carpeted with luxuriant, close-cropped grass, almost as smooth as the lawn of a city park. The trees had crowded themselves to the very edge of the greensward. They closed up on the strip of lawn like a wall and stretched their branches over it, as if to shield it from the sun.

‚ÄúDid you ever see anything so sweet in all 88 your life?‚Äù cried Ruth, as she flung herself on the turf.

‚ÄúNever!‚Äù agreed the others with enthusiasm, following her example.

‚ÄúThis pool is supposed to be haunted,‚Äù said Zerlina, and Bab started, remembering the face she had just seen.

‚ÄúHaunted by what, Zerlina?‚Äù she asked.

‚ÄúIt is not known,‚Äù replied the Gypsy girl, mysteriously; ‚Äúbut on moonlight nights some one is often seen sitting on this bank.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat some one‚Äîa man or a woman?‚Äù persisted Bab.

‚ÄúIt is not known,‚Äù repeated Zerlina. ‚ÄúBut it has been seen, nevertheless. Besides,‚Äù she continued, ‚Äúthis is supposed to be the meeting-place of fairies. Though people do not believe in fairies in this country.‚Äù

‚ÄúI do,‚Äù declared Mollie, and the other girls laughed light-heartedly.

‚ÄúAnd,‚Äù went on Zerlina, ‚Äúthe deer who live in this wood come here to graze and drink water from the pool.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow, that I can believe,‚Äù said Ruth.

‚ÄúWell, it is an enchanted spot,‚Äù cried Mollie. ‚ÄúIt must be. Look at Zerlina‚Äôs dog.‚Äù

The shepherd dog had taken his tail in his mouth and was circling slowly. The girls watched him breathlessly as he turned faster 89 and faster. Once he fell into the stream, but he never stopped and continued to circle so rapidly, as he clambered out, that he lost all sense of direction and waltzed over the girls‚Äô laps, staining their dresses with his wet feet, while they laughed until the tears rolled down their cheeks, and the woods rang with the merry sound.

At a word from the Gypsy girl the dog stopped and stretched himself exhausted, on the ground.

‚ÄúZerlina, you must have bewitched that animal,‚Äù cried Ruth. ‚ÄúBut wasn‚Äôt it beautiful? If we had been lying down he would have waltzed right over our faces.‚Äù

‚ÄúGirls,‚Äù proposed Grace, after they had recovered from the exhibition of the waltzing dog, ‚Äúlet‚Äôs go in wading.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat a great idea, Grace!‚Äù cried Ruth. In a jiffy they had their shoes and stockings piled together on the bank and had slipped into the little pool of clear, running water.

Zerlina watched them from the bank. Perhaps Miss Sallie was right, and water had no charms for this Gypsy child.

As they clung to each other, giving little shrieks of pleasure and making a great splashing, Mollie exclaimed suddenly:

‚ÄúLook, look! Here comes a man!‚Äù

Sure enough there was a man emerging from the trees on the other side of the stream. The 90 girls scampered excitedly out of the water, giggling, as girls will do, and sat in a row on the bank, tailor-fashion, hiding their wet feet under their skirts.

By this time the stranger had come up to the pool and stood gazing in amazement at the party of young women.

‚ÄúWell, for the love of Mike!‚Äù he exclaimed.

It was Jimmie Butler, one of the major‚Äôs house party.

Then he caught sight of the pyramid of shoes and stockings; his face broke into a smile and he laughed so contagiously that everybody joined in. Once more the enchanted pool was given over to merriment.

‚ÄúWhere on earth did you come from?‚Äù demanded Ruth.

‚ÄúAnd where have you been?‚Äù he echoed.

Whereupon everybody talked at once, until all the adventures had been related.

‚ÄúAnd you‚Äôre actually alive, after all these hairbreadth escapes, and able to amuse yourselves in this simple fashion?‚Äù gasped Jimmie Butler. ‚ÄúLadies, putting all joking aside, permit me to compliment you on your amazing nerve. ‚ÄúBut how did you happen to be here?‚Äù repeated Ruth.

‚ÄúOh, I‚Äôm just out for a morning stroll,‚Äù he replied. ‚ÄúI came to see the haunted pool.‚Äù

‚ÄúJust take another little stroll, for five minutes, until we get on our shoes and stockings. Then we‚Äôll all go back to our home of canvas,‚Äù said Ruth.

By the time they had reached the encampment Bab had almost forgotten about the strange face she had seen, and they were all talking happily together about Ten Eyck Hall, which, according to Jimmie Butler, was the finest old house in that part of the country.

In the meantime the major himself had arrived in his automobile, while the boys had ridden over on horseback. When the others came up, they found the chauffeur busily engaged in repairing the tires of Ruth‚Äôs automobile. Miss Stuart and Major Ten Eyck were deep in conversation, while the Gypsies stood about in groups, looking at the strangers indifferently.

‚ÄúMiss Ruth,‚Äù said the major, after greetings had been exchanged, ‚Äúif you can run this machine, suppose we start at once and leave my chauffeur to follow with yours. You ladies must be very hungry. We will have an early luncheon.‚Äù

The girls said good-bye to the Gypsies and 92 thanked them graciously. Ruth had tried to compensate Granny Ann, but the old woman had haughtily refused to accept a cent.

‚ÄúA Gypsy takes nothing from his guest,‚Äù she said, and Ruth was obliged to let the matter drop. However, she made the old Gypsy promise to bring her granddaughter over to see them very soon, and as they disappeared down the road, they saw Zerlina leaning against a tree, watching them wistfully.

At last, the journey which had been so full of peril and adventure was ended, and ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls‚Äù arrived safely at Ten Eyck Hall.

CHAPTER IX‚ÄîTEN EYCK HALL

Ten Eyck Hall, with its high-peaked roofs, its rambling wings and innumerable dormer windows, seemed to the four girls the very home of romance.

It was an enormous house built of brick, turned a faded pink, now, from age, which made a delicate background for the heavy vines that shaded the piazzas and balconies and clambered up to the roof itself.

The handsome old master of this charming house leaped to the ground as lightly as one of 93 his nephews, the moment the automobile drew up at the front door. Lifting his hat he made a low, old-fashioned bow.

‚ÄúDear ladies,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúyou are as welcome to my home as the flowers in spring!‚Äù Giving his arm to Miss Stuart, he conducted her up the front steps. ‚ÄúDear me,‚Äù exclaimed Miss Sallie, ‚ÄúI feel as if I were entering a baronial castle. Why did you never tell me years ago you owned such a fine place, John Ten Eyck?‚Äù

‚ÄúBecause I didn‚Äôt in those days, Sallie,‚Äù answered the major. ‚ÄúThere were several heirs ahead of me then. But I always wanted you to come and see it. Don‚Äôt you remember my mother wrote and asked you to make us a visit? But you were going abroad, that summer, and couldn‚Äôt come.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, I was a very foolish girl,‚Äù replied Miss Sallie. ‚ÄúBut better late than never, John, and it will be a pleasure to see the young people enjoy themselves in this beautiful house.‚Äù

‚ÄúI feel like a princess in a castle, Major,‚Äù called Ruth.

‚ÄúMartinez is the son of an old friend of mine, Jos√© Martinez of Madrid,‚Äù broke in the major. ‚ÄúBut how did you happen to meet him?‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, well,‚Äù laughed Major Ten Eyck, ‚Äúwe won‚Äôt embarrass you any more by insisting on your being a hero whether you will or no. Here comes Mary to show you to your rooms, ladies. The major waved his hand gallantly to the five women who were following the housekeeper up the carved oak staircase to the regions above.

‚ÄúAm I awake, or asleep?‚Äù asked Mollie. ‚ÄúThis whole morning has seemed like a dream, and now this lovely old house‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd the lovely old major, in the lovely old house,‚Äù added Ruth.

By this time they had reached the top of the stairs and had a glimpse of another hall corresponding to the one below, at one end of which was a great casement window with a broad cushioned window-seat under it. The other end, where the stairs turned, was lighted by an enormous stained glass window.

Little exclamations of rapture escaped the girls as they tripped over the softly carpeted floors to their rooms, which were on the left side of the hall. Opposite were the major‚Äôs rooms, so Mary explained, while the young men were all quartered in the right wing except Mr. Martinez, who had a room at the end of the hall on the same side as the major‚Äôs suite.

‚ÄúI could live and die in a house like this, and never want to leave it,‚Äù cried Bab, her eyes sparkling with pleasure as Mary opened the door leading to the room that had been assigned to Ruth and her.

They could have a room apiece, if they wished it, the housekeeper said, but when it was discovered that this would necessitate two of the girls taking rooms in the right wing, many passages and corridors away from the others, all said they would rather share the rooms on the main hall. Mary looked somewhat relieved at this. It was 98 evident she was not in favor of the right wing for the girls, either; although she did not explain her reasons.

In the large old-fashioned bedrooms, hung with chintz curtains and furnished with mahogany that would have been the joy of the antique dealers, were already placed the boxes and satchels of the automobilists. ‚ÄúWell, that seems a good deal to complete the picture, considering he was wearing an ordinary pepper and salt suit,‚Äù observed Barbara.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt believe you like Senor Jos√© Martinez,‚Äù said Ruth.

‚ÄúOh, yes I do,‚Äù replied the other. ‚ÄúI like 99 him and I don‚Äôt like him. His eyes are just a bit too close together, and still he is very handsome. But give me time, give me time. ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt use too much of it,‚Äù called Ruth. ‚ÄúI never saw a country house where the water didn‚Äôt run short, no matter how grand a place it was. Remember the drought, Bab, and leave a little for your fainting friend.‚Äù

The girls had barely time to bathe and dress, when a deep gong sounded in the hall. The five automobilists, refreshed by their belated baths, and dainty in crisp ducks and muslins, filed down the great staircase at the sound. Miss Stuart, in a lavender organdie, her white hair piled on top of her head, led the procession. 100

The major, waiting for them at the foot of the steps, smiled rather sadly as he watched the charming picture. The five young men grouped together at the end of the hall, came forward at sight of the ladies. Three of them at least were rather shy in their greetings, especially the English boy, Alfred Marsdale, who was only seventeen and still afraid of American girls. Stephen and Martin Ten Eyck, boys of sixteen and seventeen, were also rather green in the society of girls. They had no sisters and their vacations had been spent either at Ten Eyck Hall or out West on their father‚Äôs ranch. Jimmie Butler, who was nineteen and had seen a deal of life all over the world with his mother, a well-to-do widow, was proof against embarrassment, and the young Spaniard also seemed perfectly at his ease.

‚ÄúCome along, young people,‚Äù said the major, giving his arm to Miss Sallie and leading the way to the dining room.

Soon they were all gayly chatting at an immense, round table of black oak, so highly polished that it reflected the silver and china and the faces of the guests in its shining board. 101

‚ÄúMiss Barbara,‚Äù said the major, ‚Äúsuppose you let us have a history of the attempt at robbery? ‚ÄúHe was not a giant,‚Äù answered Barbara. ‚ÄúHe struck me as being rather short and very slender, so slender that it made him appear taller than he was. His voice was curious. ‚ÄúDo you suppose he‚Äôs hiding in the woods now, Major?‚Äù asked Mollie. ‚ÄúWe were walking there all morning, but we had nothing to be robbed of.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, he is probably running still,‚Äù replied the major. ‚ÄúBut what is quite plain to me is that it was somebody who knew you expected to make the trip. Barbara blushed.

‚ÄúI am sure any of the others would have done the same thing, Major, if Mr. Stuart had given them the pistol.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy, certainly, Alfred, my boy,‚Äù replied Jimmie Butler. ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you know it‚Äôs dangerous, in this country, for a woman to walk on the streets unarmed unless she is dressed like 103 a suffragette? And then she doesn‚Äôt need a pistol to make people run from her.‚Äù

‚ÄúNow, you‚Äôre joking, Jimmie,‚Äù said Alfred.

At which everybody laughed until they all felt that they had known each other much longer than just a few hours.

‚ÄúWhile I think of it,‚Äù observed the major, ‚ÄúI have only one request to make of my guests, and that may seem like a very inhospitable one, but you will all understand, I know. Don‚Äôt be too lavish with the water.‚Äù

Ruth and Barbara looked at each other and smiled.

‚ÄúI mean,‚Äù continued the major, ‚Äúdon‚Äôt fill the tubs to the brim. A hand‚Äôs depth is the allowance; or we shall be high and dry without any water and no prospect of any unless a rain comes. This interminable drought has dried up every brook on the place and the cisterns are lower than they have ever been before. We keep one cistern always full‚Äînot so much in case of drought as in case of fire; it might be needed some day.‚Äù

They all promised to bathe in what Jimmie Butler called ‚Äútwo-fingers of water.‚Äù

‚ÄúIf the water gives out,‚Äù said Jimmie, ‚Äúwe‚Äôll beautify our complexions by bathing in milk. I think I need a lotion for a delicate skin, anyhow.‚Äù Jimmie‚Äôs nose was a mass of freckles. 104

‚ÄúYou would have to have your face peeled, Jimmie,‚Äù said Stephen, ‚Äúbefore you could call it delicate.‚Äù

‚ÄúExcuse me,‚Äù replied Jimmie, ‚Äúmy indelicate skin then.‚Äù

‚ÄúI have not made any plans for your entertainment this afternoon, young ladies,‚Äù the major was saying. ‚ÄúMiss Stuart is determined that you must lie down and sleep off the effects of the Gypsy camp. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not a bit tired now,‚Äù said Ruth.

‚ÄúNeither are we,‚Äù echoed the other girls as they rose from the table.

‚ÄúWell, suppose we make a compromise,‚Äù said the major, ‚Äúby showing you over the house? After that sleep must be your portion, eh, Sallie?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt must, indeed,‚Äù replied that lady firmly, and all adjourned to the library.

105CHAPTER X‚ÄîAN ATTIC MYSTERY

The library of Ten Eyck Hall was, to Bab, the most beautiful of all the rooms. The walls were literally lined with books from floor to ceiling, and there were little galleries halfway up for the convenience of getting books that were too high to reach from the floor. Big leather chairs and couches were scattered about and heavy curtains seemed to conceal entrances to mysterious doors and passages leading off somewhere into the depths of the old house.

‚ÄúThis is just the place for a secret door or a staircase in the wall,‚Äù exclaimed Grace.

‚ÄúThere is a secret door, I believe, in this very room,‚Äù replied the major; ‚Äúbut it is really a secret, for the location was lost long ago and nobody has ever been able to find it since.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow interesting!‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúCan‚Äôt you thump the walls and locate it by a hollow sound?‚Äù

‚ÄúPress a panel, my boy. That is all that is 106 necessary,‚Äù replied Jimmie. ‚ÄúWith a wild shriek Lady Gwendolyn rushed through the portals of the lofty chamber. The panel was restored to its place and Sir Marmanduke and her pursuers were foiled.‚Äù

All this, the irrepressible Jimmie had acted out with wild gesticulations.

‚ÄúWake up, Al, old man! What‚Äôs the matter with you?‚Äù

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll have to take turn about playing,‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúGrace and I each play a little.‚Äù

‚ÄúJimmie, you‚Äôre a brick,‚Äù said Alfred.

‚ÄúThis is the prettiest room of all, I think,‚Äù observed Mollie.

‚ÄúWho wants to see the attic?‚Äù said Martin.

‚ÄúI never even tried the experiment, Sallie,‚Äù replied the gentle old soldier, ‚Äúbecause I always found it harder on me than on the boys. It‚Äôs really a certain sort of selfishness on my part, 108 I suppose. Cut along now, boys, and don‚Äôt keep the girls from their rest too long.‚Äù

The pilgrimage started up the great front staircase, led by Martin and his older brother, who together had made many excursions to the attic and knew the way by heart.

On the second floor the explorers followed a passage that led to another flight of stairs, and this in turn to another passage, and finally to one last narrow flight of steps with a mysterious door at the top.

‚ÄúThis reminds me of the House of Usher,‚Äù said Jimmie, ‚Äúonly it goes up instead of down. Can‚Äôt you imagine all these doors opening and closing, and the sound of footsteps on the stairs, down, down?‚Äù

Just then Martin opened the door and a gust of wind blew in their faces. Something flashed past that almost made the whole party fall backwards down the steps.

Mollie gave a little shriek.

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt be frightened,‚Äù said Jos√©, who was standing just behind her. ‚ÄúIt is only a bird.‚Äù

Lights have been seen at midnight, shining through some of these windows, and the only persons who are 109 not afraid are the housekeeper and the butler, who come twice a year, and clean out the dust.‚Äù

The young people found themselves in a vast attic whose edges were hidden by dense shadows. The center was lighted by dormer windows, here and there, that gleamed like so many eyes from the high sloping roof. Scattered about were all sorts of odds and ends of antiquated furniture, chests of drawers, hair trunks, carved boxes and spinning wheels.

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt this great!‚Äù cried Jimmie Butler. ‚ÄúJust the place for handsprings,‚Äù and he began to turn somersaults like a professional, while the girls looked on delighted.

‚ÄúStop that, Jim,‚Äù protested Stephen. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôll get yourself filthy and break your neck into the bargain. You are much too old for such child‚Äôs play. You‚Äôll have rush of blood to the head and strain a nerve, and heaven knows you‚Äôve got enough to strain.‚Äù

    ‚Äú‚ÄòIn  my  youth,  Father  William  replied  to  his  son,
    I  feared  it  would  injure  the  brain,

    Why,  I  do  it  again  and  again!‚Äô‚Äù

sang Jimmie as he wheeled over the floor toward a partition wall which cut off one end of the great room. Over and over he circled, without looking where he was going, until suddenly, bang, his heels hit against the wall. 110

There was a curious grating noise, a creaking of rafters, and before their amazed eyes the wall slid along and disclosed another attic as large as the first.

Jimmie was so bewildered he forgot to pull himself up from the dusty floor, and lay with his head propped against an old trunk looking across the enormous space.

Then everybody began talking at once.

‚ÄúThis looks to me like smugglers,‚Äù cried Alfred. ‚ÄúI was in an old house in England, where there was the same sort of wall, only not so large.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, is it not curious,‚Äù replied Jos√©, ‚Äúthat they are going away from the wall and not approaching it? He must have walked out of the wall. Perhaps there is a secret door there, too.‚Äù

They rushed across pell mell, and began thumping the walls, but nothing happened.

‚ÄúI say, Stephen,‚Äù said Martin, ‚Äúdo you suppose we had smugglers in our family?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know,‚Äù answered Stephen. ‚ÄúThey managed to keep it secret if they had.‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know,‚Äù replied Jimmie. ‚ÄúI must have kicked the panel that worked the spring. Let‚Äôs see if we can move it back again. ‚ÄúAll is as if it never had been,‚Äù exclaimed Jimmie. ‚ÄúNow let‚Äôs find the place I kicked.‚Äù

‚ÄúI say, girls,‚Äù cried Jimmie, ‚Äúwouldn‚Äôt it be fun to give a fancy dress party some day, and surprise the major and Miss Stuart?‚Äù

All this time Jos√© had never left the partition, but had kept up a continuous thumping to find the sliding panel.

‚ÄúEverybody take a hand, and we will carry down everything we can find, and then we won‚Äôt have to make another trip,‚Äù called Stephen. ‚ÄúCome, Jos√©, we‚Äôre going to dress up. You‚Äôll have to be a pirate. Here‚Äôs a red sash and a three cornered hat that will just suit your style.‚Äù

She confessed to herself, as she descended the stairs, that she was rather tired. When she reached the cool, darkened library, she sat down for a moment in one of the big chairs and closed her eyes. It was very restful in there. The sun had left that side of the house in the shade and the room with its heavy hangings, its dark leather furniture and rich rugs was full of shadows.

She was almost asleep, a slender little figure in a great armchair of carved black oak. Her head dropped to one side and her eyes closed, when she was awakened with a start by a draught of cold air. A dizziness came over Barbara. She closed her eyes for a moment, and, when she opened them again, the face, or phantom, or whatever it was, had gone.

Holding her breath to keep from crying out, Barbara ran from the room as fast as her trembling knees could carry her. In the hall she met Jos√©. He looked at her curiously.

‚ÄúMademoiselle, have you seen a ghost?‚Äù he asked as he stood aside to let her pass.

She was afraid to answer, for fear of bursting into tears.

‚ÄúI am sorry,‚Äù he continued. ‚ÄúHas anything really happened?‚Äù

But still she refused to speak, and ran up the stairs.

By the time she reached her room, Bab had 115 regained her self-composure, and had again determined to say nothing about the adventure. It would only frighten the girls and take away from the pleasure of the visit.

CHAPTER XI‚ÄîJOS√â HAS AN ENEMY

    ‚ÄúI  like  them  all,  the  pretty  girls,

The young Spaniard was very graceful and lithe. He seemed to have forgotten that he was on the piazza of foreigners in a strange country. The dance grew quicker and quicker. ‚ÄúSticking in the wall of course,‚Äù replied Stephen.

‚ÄúMystery of mysteries!‚Äù cried Jimmie, ‚Äúthe knife is gone!‚Äù

‚ÄúIt is impossible,‚Äù exclaimed the major. ‚ÄúWe have not left this room. We could see anyone who came upon the piazza.‚Äù 120

‚ÄúWell, it‚Äôs gone,‚Äù said Jimmie. ‚ÄúWhile you were nursing Jos√©, somebody must have crept up and got it.‚Äù

‚ÄúGood heavens!‚Äù exclaimed Miss Sallie. ‚ÄúDo you mean to say that the murderer has been that close to us again? Do close those windows and draw the curtains.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, do so,‚Äù said the major. ‚ÄúMary,‚Äù he continued to the housekeeper, who was entering at that moment with a basin of water, ‚ÄúI wish you would have all the men on the place sent to me. Some of them may be asleep, but wake them up. We shall scour every part of the estate to-night. If there‚Äôs anybody hiding around here we shall rout him out.‚Äù

Mary hurried off to deliver her orders, while the boys ran to their rooms to get on tennis shoes and collect various weapons.

‚ÄúI am sorry Jos√© was scratched,‚Äù Martin confided to Alfred, ‚Äúbut‚Äîwell, this is pretty good sport, old man. Don‚Äôt you think so?‚Äù

‚ÄúBy Jove, it is,‚Äù replied Alfred with enthusiasm. ‚ÄúIf that assassin should leap at us in the dark I should like to give him a nip with this shillalah. What a beastly coward he was to attack a man when his back was turned!‚Äù

And with that, he waved a big knotted club, one of Stephen‚Äôs possessions, around his head, and glared ferociously. 121

‚ÄúCome on, boys,‚Äù called Stephen. ‚ÄúWe haven‚Äôt a moment to lose. The man will be well away if we don‚Äôt hurry. We are going to ride in twos and divide the place in sections.‚Äù

In another ten minutes a company of horsemen rode off in the moonlight, two by two, while the frightened maid-servants locked and barred the house doors and windows.

Jos√© had begged to be allowed to go along, but the major had silenced him by saying that Miss Sallie and the girls needed a protector, and that under the circumstances it was better for him to stay at home and look after them. Even the old major was rather enjoying the zest of a man-hunt, and his eyes flashed with a new fire under his grizzled eyebrows.

But nothing happened and the assassin remained at large. The hunters scoured the country, searched the forest on the outskirts of the Ten Eyck estate, and woke the sleeping Gypsies to demand what they knew. The Gypsies knew nothing, and at midnight the horsemen returned.

The house was silent. Everyone had gone to bed except Jos√©, who sat in the library listening for every sound that creaked through the old place. He met Major Ten Eyck and the boys at the front door, holding a candle high and peering 122 anxiously into the dark to see what quarry they had brought home.

And, when he saw they had no prisoner bound to the horse with the ropes that the major had ordered his man to take along, a look of strange relief came into the Spaniard‚Äôs face. He breathed a deep sigh, smiled as he thanked them, said good-night and went up the broad stairway with the same smile still clinging to his lips.

In the meantime Bab was stretched out beside the sleeping Ruth, wide awake, going over the events of that tumultuous day.

She felt that these events had no connection with each other, and yet deep down in her inner consciousness she was searching for the link that bound all the strange happenings together. She was not quite sure now whether she had seen the face in the library or not. She had been so tired and hot. It might, after all, have been a dream. But the footsteps in the dust on the attic floor, coming from the wall, what of them?

And last, though most strange and mysterious of all, the two daggers? Jos√© had been saved just in time from the stigma of suspicion by the appearance of the other dagger, for, in the moment she had seen the two, Bab had realized they were absolutely alike.

She could not believe Jos√© was a highwayman, and yet there were certain things that 123 looked very black. It was true he had not known where they were going, but she imagined he could have found it out.

Was it his figure she had seen behind the curtain that morning, listening? Whoever it was heard the exact route of their trip, with explicit directions from the major. Undoubtedly, Bab believed, the eavesdropper was the highwayman.

Furthermore, what did they know about Jos√©? It is true he had come bearing credentials, but such things were easily fixed up by experts, and the major was a simple old fellow who never doubted anybody until he had to.

On the other hand, Jos√© had every appearance of being a gentleman. He had proved himself to be brave by knocking down the tramp twice his size at Sleepy Hollow. There was an air of sincerity about him which she could not fail to recognize. He was graceful and charming. Everybody liked him, even those who had been inclined to feel prejudiced at first.

Would the Spaniard have dared to use the same dagger in the dance that he had used to slash their tires with? It was assuredly amazingly reckless, and yet he might have trusted to the darkness and risked it.

But the look he gave her when she started to speak of the twin daggers! What could that 124 have meant? Was he trying to shield his own enemy?

Should she speak to the major or should she say nothing?

On the whole, Barbara thought it would be better to keep quiet for a day or two. It might be that Miss Sallie would insist on taking them away after this last attack; but she believed Ruth‚Äôs and the major‚Äôs prayers would prevail, and that they would all stay through the visit.

They had planned so many delightful parties it seemed a shame to break up on the very first day of their visit. And, after all, Miss Sallie had a great tenderness for the major, a tenderness lasting through thirty years.

Then Barbara dropped off to sleep, and in the old house only one other soul was still awake as the clock in the hall chimed the hour of two.

In his room, by the light of a flickering candle, Jos√© sat examining the dagger that had so baffled Bab‚Äôs curiosity. On his face was an expression of sorrow and bitterness that would certainly have aroused her pity had she seen him that moment. At last he shook his head hopelessly, muttered something in Spanish, and blew out the candle.

But before getting into bed he picked up the dagger again.

‚ÄúEven in America,‚Äù he said in English, 125 ‚Äúeven in this far country it is the same. But I will not endure it,‚Äù he muttered. ‚ÄúIt is too much!‚Äù

Putting his dagger under the pillow, he crept to bed.

CHAPTER XII‚ÄîNOSEGAYS AND TENNIS

The household was late in pulling itself together next morning. At half-past nine, Mary and her husband, John, had carried trays of coffee and rolls to the rooms of the guests, informing them, at the same time, that luncheon would be served at half-past twelve.

Mollie and Grace, in dressing gowns and slippers, had carried their trays into the room shared by Ruth and Barbara. Miss Sallie had followed, looking so charming in her lavender silk wrapper, elaborately trimmed with lace and ribbons that all the girls had exclaimed with admiration; which put the lady in a very good humor at the outset. Who does not like to be complimented, especially in the early morning when one is not apt to feel at one‚Äôs best?

To add to the gayety of the company there was a knock on the door, which, when opened, 126 disclosed John bearing a large tray of flowers, a small nosegay for each of the girls and a large bunch of dewy sweet peas for Miss Sallie, all with the major‚Äôs compliments.

‚ÄúWhat a man he is!‚Äù she cried. ‚ÄúHe disarms me with his bunches of flowers just as I was about to tell him something very disagreeable. I really don‚Äôt see how I can do it.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs true,‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúYet by the magic Rule of Three we should have no more. We have finished now and the curse is lifted.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen Mollie‚Äôs old Gypsy comes over we must ask her to tell a few things,‚Äù observed Grace. ‚ÄúI believe she really can predict the future. ‚ÄúHe seems to be a very remarkable young man,‚Äù said Miss Sallie. ‚ÄúDid you notice he never said one word? Just sat there as quietly as if nothing had happened.‚Äù

But nobody had, evidently. They had all been too excited and horror-struck at the time to have noticed anything. 128

‚ÄúI saw it was a knife, and that was all,‚Äù said Ruth.

‚ÄúI never saw a man dance before,‚Äù observed Mollie, as if following aloud a train of thoughts she had been pursuing while the others talked. ‚ÄúI was almost sorry he said he would, but when I saw what kind of dancing it was I was glad. ‚ÄúRuth,‚Äù she said, ‚Äúdo you know, the smell of the honeysuckles gives me the queerest sensation? I feel as if I had been here before, once long ago, ever so long. The girls found the two boys waiting in the hall to keep their appointment. Alfred was fast losing his shyness in the presence of these two wholesome and unaffected girls who could play tennis almost as well as he could, ride horseback, run a motor car, repel a highwayman with a pistol and not lose their heads when they needed to keep them most. But, what was more to the purpose, they were not in the least shy or afraid to speak out. ‚ÄúYou can get in the next set, Jimmie,‚Äù called 132 Stephen. ‚ÄúWe are just starting in on this one.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt sounds fine,‚Äù said Jimmie, ‚Äúif you mean the haunted pool. It is a beautiful spot, and it has a new haunt since last you saw it, Major. It‚Äôs haunted by water nymphs now.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre likely to meet Jimmie anywhere,‚Äù said Martin. ‚ÄúHe‚Äôs a regular Johnnie-on-the-spot. He is the first person to get up and the last one to go to bed. ‚ÄúJos√©,‚Äù asked the major, putting his arm through the young Spaniard‚Äôs, ‚Äúhave you any theories about last night?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù replied Jos√©. ‚ÄúI do not think it will do any good to hunt for the one who threw the knife. I have, in my country, an enemy. I believe it was he.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat?‚Äù cried the major. ‚ÄúHe has followed you all the way to America, and your life is constantly in danger?‚Äù

‚ÄúI do not think he will come again,‚Äù answered Jos√©. ‚ÄúAt any rate, I am not afraid,‚Äù he added, shrugging his shoulders, ‚Äúand I can do nothing.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, Madam, I could. But it would not be easy to catch him.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo more dangerous than America, Madam, I find,‚Äù replied Jos√©.

‚ÄúTrue enough,‚Äù assented Miss Sallie, ‚Äúsince this is America and not Spain, and we find ourselves 135 in a perfect hotbed of criminals. My dear John, I think we shall need a body-guard if we go out in the open this afternoon.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, Sallie,‚Äù answered the courteous old man, ‚Äúyou shall have one in me and my nephews and their friends‚Äîa devoted body-guard, I assure you.‚Äù

At luncheon the feeling of good will which comes to friends who have just found each other, so to speak, had spread itself. Enjoyment was in the air and there were no discordant elements. All their troubles were of the past, and Bab determined to cast aside her suspicions and regard Jos√© in the light of a mysterious but otherwise exceedingly attractive foreigner. When she looked across the table into his clear, brown eyes, which regarded her sadly but without a single guilty quiver of the lids, she could not but believe that there had been some bitter mistake somewhere. He was lonely and strange, and there was something about him that aroused her pity. Everybody liked him; even Miss Sallie was attracted by his graceful and gentle manners.

Luncheon over, everyone made ready for the auto trip, and it was not long before the two autos carrying a merry party, had set forth.

136CHAPTER XIII‚ÄîCROSS QUESTIONS AND CROOKED ANSWERS

After a long ride through the country, skirting the edge of the forest in which the highwayman had lurked, and where the smoke from the Gypsies‚Äô camp fire could be seen curling up in the distance, the two automobiles took to the river road.

Ruth was steering her own car with Alfred beside her; behind them on the small seat sat Jos√© and Mollie, and on the back seat were Bab and Stephen. As they skimmed over the bridge, which had been repaired by the major‚Äôs men, Mollie said to Jos√©:

‚ÄúWas the bridge all right, Mr. Martinez, when you came over it the other day?‚Äù

The Spaniard flushed and his eye caught Bab‚Äôs, who was gazing at him curiously.

‚ÄúYes, no‚Äîor rather, I do not know,‚Äù he stammered. ‚ÄúI did not come by the bridge but through the forest.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut how did you find the way?‚Äù asked Mollie, wondering a little at his embarrassment.

‚ÄúI asked it,‚Äù he replied, ‚Äúof a Gypsy.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, really?‚Äù cried Mollie. ‚ÄúAnd did she tell you?‚Äù 137

‚ÄúIt was not a woman,‚Äù went on Jos√©. ‚ÄúIt was a man.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd did he know the way? Because they told us they did not, perhaps because they didn‚Äôt want to be disturbed so late in the evening.‚Äù

‚ÄúPerhaps,‚Äù said Jos√©, and changed the subject by asking Stephen whose was the large estate they were now approaching. It was that of a famous millionaire, and their attention was for the moment distracted. Jos√© seemed to breath a sigh of relief and engaged Mollie in conversation for the rest of the ride, telling her about his own country, the bull fights and carnivals and a hundred other things of interest until the little girl had quite forgotten his confusion at the mention of the damaged bridge.

On the way back the automobiles turned into the wooded road, but before they reached the Gypsy camp they turned again into another road pointed out by Martin in the first car. The road led directly through the forest to the haunted pool, where the automobiles drew up. The pool, in the late afternoon sunlight, was more enchanting than ever.

‚ÄúThis is a famous spot in the neighborhood,‚Äù observed the major. ‚ÄúWhen I was a boy it was the scene of many a picnic and frolic. People in these parts were more neighborly in those days. The girls and boys used to meet and ride 138 in wagons or on horseback over here. We ate our luncheons on this mossy bank; then strolled about in couples until dark and drove home by moonlight.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe Gypsy girl told us it was really haunted, Major,‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúShe even said she had seen the ghost.‚Äù

‚ÄúIndeed,‚Äù replied the major, looking up a little startled, ‚Äúand what sort of ghost was it?‚Äù

‚ÄúJust a figure sitting here on the bank,‚Äù answered Ruth.

‚ÄúOh!‚Äù he exclaimed in a tone of evident relief.

‚ÄúWhy, Major,‚Äù cried Miss Sallie, ‚Äúone would think you believed in ghosts.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd so I do, Sallie, my dear,‚Äù declared the gentle old major, ‚Äúbut only in the ghosts of my lost youth, which seem to appear to me to-day in the forms of all these delightful young people. What about tea, Miss Ruth Stuart?‚Äù he demanded, turning to Ruth.

The chauffeur brought out the elaborate tea basket which had served them so well at the Gypsy camp and Ruth and Barbara proceeded to make the tea while the other girls unpacked boxes of delicious sandwiches and tea cakes.

‚ÄúThis is a very beautiful spot,‚Äù observed Jos√©. ‚ÄúIf it were perpetual summer I could live and die on this mossy bank and never tire 139 of it!‚Äù Walking a little apart from the others he stretched himself out at full length on the ground, staring up into the branches overhead.

Then the other boys, who had been strolling about under the trees, returned, but they were not alone. They had espied Zerlina in the depths of the woods, with her guitar slung over her shoulder, and persuaded her to go back with them to the pool.

‚ÄúYou see we‚Äôve brought a wandering minstrel with us,‚Äù cried Jimmie. ‚ÄúShe has promised to sing us a song of the Romany Rye, haven‚Äôt you, Zerlina?‚Äù

The girls greeted Zerlina cordially. She was presented to the major, but Jos√©, as she approached, had turned over on his side and flung his arm over his head, as if he were asleep.

‚ÄúLeave him alone. He‚Äôs dreaming,‚Äù said Jimmie. ‚ÄúGive Zerlina some tea and cake, and then we‚Äôll have a song.‚Äù

Zerlina ate the cake greedily and drank her tea in silence. She examined the fresh summer dresses of ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls,‚Äù and a look of envy came into her eyes as she cast them down on her cotton skirt full of tatters from the briars and faded from red into a soft old pink shade. But she was very pretty, even in her ragged dress, which was turned in at the collar showing her full, rounded throat and shapely 140 neck. She was lithe and graceful, and as she thrummed on the guitar with her slender, brown fingers her ragged dress and rough shoes faded into insignificance. The group of people sitting on the bank saw only a beautiful, dark-haired girl with a glowing face and eyes that shone with a smouldering fire. After a few preliminary chords she began to sing in a rich contralto voice. The song again was in the Romany tongue. It seemed to convey to the listeners a note of sadness and loneliness.

The kind old major was much impressed by the performance.

‚ÄúZerlina,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúyou have a very beautiful voice, much too beautiful to be wasted. You must ask your grandmother to bring you over to Ten Eyck Hall. I should like to hear you sing again.‚Äù

‚ÄúZerlina will be a great opera singer, one of these days,‚Äù predicted Jimmie. ‚ÄúShe will be singing Carmen, yet, at the Manhattan Opera House. How would you like that, Zerlina?‚Äù

The Gypsy girl made no reply. Her eyes were fastened on Jos√©, who still lay as if asleep, his back turned to the circle.

‚ÄúShe can dance, too,‚Äù cried Ruth. ‚ÄúShe told me she could. This would be a pretty place to dance, Zerlina, where the fairies dance by moonlight.‚Äù 141

‚ÄúI have no music,‚Äù objected Zerlina.

‚ÄúOh, I can make the music all right,‚Äù said the irrepressible Jimmie, seizing the guitar and tuning it up. Then he began to whistle. The tone was clear and flute-like and the tune the same Spanish dance he had played for Jos√©. Zerlina pricked up her ears when she heard the music and the rhythm of the guitar. It is said that no Gypsy can ever resist the sound of music. Now the body of the girl began swaying to the beat of the accompaniment. Presently she began to dance, a real Spanish dance full of gestures and movement. They half guessed the story woven in, a lover repelled and called back, coquetted with and threatened; threatened with a knife which she drew from the blouse of her dress and then restored to its hiding place; for the dance ended quickly without disaster, imaginary or otherwise. Miss Sallie had given a little cry at sight of another murderous weapon. But the knife! Had no one seen it, no one recognized the chased silver handle and the slightly curved blade? Bab sat as if rooted to the spot, waiting for somebody to speak, to cry out that the knife was the same that had whizzed past Jos√©‚Äôs head the other night. After all, nobody had really seen it but herself. She had learned by a former experience to keep her own counsel, and she decided to 142 wait, and not to tell until matters took a more definite turn.

Was it possible this beautiful Gypsy girl could be a murderess, or one at heart? But, on the other hand, would she have dared to display the mysterious dagger in the presence of the same company? Bab was puzzled and worried. Was Zerlina a robber also, or was Jos√©, after all, the robber? Perhaps there was some connection between them. There must be, since they had exchanged knives on several occasions.

Her reflections were interrupted by a general movement toward the automobiles. Zerlina was evidently pleased at the praises she had received, for her cheeks were flushed with pride.

‚ÄúWon‚Äôt you let us see your dagger, Zerlina?‚Äù asked Bab.

‚ÄúI cannot, now,‚Äù she said. ‚ÄúPerhaps, another time. Good-bye.‚Äù She slipped off into the woods as quietly as one of the spirits which were said to haunt the place.

‚ÄúIf you had ever read any books on Gypsies, Sallie,‚Äù replied the major, ‚Äúyou would know that their lives are full of things they must keep secret if they want to keep out of jail. ‚ÄúWhy, where‚Äôs Mr. Martinez?‚Äù asked Mollie, as they climbed into the automobiles and she missed her companion of the ride over.

One of the boys gave a shrill whistle and the others began calling and shouting. Presently the answer came from up the stream. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm 144 coming,‚Äù he called and Jos√© appeared. ‚ÄúI was only taking a little stroll.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhy did you wish to miss the Gypsy song and dance?‚Äù demanded Mollie. ‚ÄúIt was charming.‚Äù

‚ÄúPardon, Mademoiselle,‚Äù he replied, stiffly, ‚Äúbut I do not care to hear the songs of my country, or to see its dances in a foreign land.‚Äù

Mollie was a little piqued by Jos√©‚Äôs short answer, but she forgave him when he said sadly:

‚ÄúBut I thought you said you liked America?‚Äù she persisted.

‚ÄúSo I do,‚Äù he replied; ‚Äúnevertheless, there are times when I feel very lonely. You will forgive me, will you not. Was I rude?‚Äù

In the meantime Stephen said to Barbara:

‚ÄúBab, are you a good walker? How would you like to take a short cut through the woods to-morrow morning, and visit the hermit who lives on the other side? ‚ÄúI am good for a ten mile walk,‚Äù replied Barbara. ‚ÄúI have done it many a time at home.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe woods grow more and more interesting the deeper you go into them,‚Äù continued Stephen. ‚ÄúThere are places where the sun never comes through, and the whole way is cool and shaded. ‚ÄúHow interesting!‚Äù exclaimed Bab. ‚ÄúI hope Miss Sallie won‚Äôt object.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe shall have to get the major on our side,‚Äù replied Stephen, ‚Äúand perhaps win her over, too.‚Äù 146

‚ÄúOh, she is not really so strict,‚Äù replied Bab, ‚Äúbut she feels the responsibility of looking after other peoples‚Äô children, she says.‚Äù

‚ÄúHere we are,‚Äù said Stephen, as the cars stopped at Ten Eyck Hall.

CHAPTER XIV‚ÄîIN THE DEEP WOODS

It was not such a difficult matter, after all, to win permission from Miss Sallie and the major to take the walk through the forest. The major explained to Miss Sallie that Stephen was a safe and careful guide who knew the country by heart, and that if the girls were equal to the walk there would be no danger in the excursion. The party, however, dwindled to five persons, Bab and Ruth, Stephen, Jimmie and Alfred. The latter appeared early, equipped for the walk, carrying a heavy cane, his trousers turned up over stout boots.

‚ÄúNow, Stephen,‚Äù said Miss Sallie, ‚ÄúI want you to promise me to take good care of the girls. You say the woods are not dangerous, although a highwayman stepped out of them one evening and attacked us with a knife. But I take your word for it, since the major says it is safe and I see Alfred is armed.‚Äù 147

Everybody laughed at this, and Alfred looked conscious and blushed.

‚ÄúDoesn‚Äôt one carry a cane in this country?‚Äù he asked.

‚ÄúNot often at your age, my boy,‚Äù replied Jimmie. ‚ÄúBut I daresay it will serve to beat a trail through the underbrush.‚Äù

‚ÄúCome along, girls; let‚Äôs be off,‚Äù cried Stephen, who at heart was almost a Gypsy, and loved a long tramp through the woods. He had strapped over his shoulder a goodly sized box of lunch, and the cavalcade started cheerfully down the walk that led toward the forest, a compact mass of foliage lying to the left of them.

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt this fun?‚Äù demanded Jimmie. ‚ÄúI feel just in the humor for a lark.‚Äù

‚ÄúI hope you can climb fences, girls,‚Äù called Stephen over his shoulder, as he trudged along, ahead of the others.

‚ÄúWe could even climb a tree if we had to,‚Äù answered Bab, ‚Äúor swim a creek.‚Äù

‚ÄúOr ride a horse bareback,‚Äù interrupted Jimmie, who had heard the story of Bab‚Äôs escapade on the road to Newport.

‚ÄúThis is the end of uncle‚Äôs land,‚Äù said Stephen, at last. ‚ÄúWe now find ourselves entering the black forest. Here‚Äôs the trail,‚Äù he called as the others helped the two girls over the dividing fence. 148

‚ÄúAll right, Scout Stephen,‚Äù replied Jimmie. ‚ÄúWe are following close behind. Proceed with the march.‚Äù

Sure enough, there was a distinct road leading straight into the forest, formed by ruts from cartwheels, probably the carts of the woodcutters, Stephen explained. The edges of the wood were rather thin and scant, like the meagre fringe on a man‚Äôs head just beginning to turn bald at the temples; but as they marched deeper into the forest, the trees grew so thickly that their branches overhead formed a canopy like a roof. Squirrels and chipmunks scampered across their path and occasionally a rabbit could be seen scurrying through the underbrush.

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt this great!‚Äù exclaimed Stephen, after they had been walking for some time. ‚ÄúUncle says there‚Äôs scarcely such another wood in this part of the country.‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt speak so loud, Stephen,‚Äù said Jimmie. ‚ÄúIt is so quiet here, I feel as if we would wake something, if we spoke above a whisper.‚Äù

‚ÄúLet‚Äôs wake the echoes,‚Äù replied Stephen and he gave a yodel familiar to all boys, a sort of trilling in the head and throat that is melodious in sound and carries further than an ordinary call. Immediately there was an answer to the yodel. It might have seemed an echo, only there was no place for an echo in this shut-in spot. 149

They all stopped and listened as the answer died away among the branches of the trees.

‚ÄúCurious,‚Äù said Jimmie. ‚ÄúIt was rather close, too. Perhaps one of your woodcutters is playing a trick on us, Stephen. Suppose we try again, and see what happens!‚Äù Jimmie gave another yodel, louder and longer than the first. As they paused and listened, the answer came again like an echo, this time even nearer.

‚ÄúLet‚Äôs investigate,‚Äù proposed Alfred. ‚ÄúI think it came from over there,‚Äù and he led the way through the trees toward the echo.

‚ÄúHalloo-o,‚Äù he called, ‚Äúwho are you?‚Äù and the answer came back ‚ÄúHalloo-o, who are you?‚Äù followed by a mocking laugh.

‚ÄúWell, after all, it isn‚Äôt any of our business who you are,‚Äù cried Stephen, exasperated, ‚Äúand I don‚Äôt think we had better leave the trail just here for a fellow who is afraid to come out and show himself,‚Äù he added in a lower tone.

There was no reply and they returned to the cartwheel road and began the march again.

‚ÄúYou were quite right, Stephen,‚Äù said Ruth, ‚Äúwhy should we waste our time over an idler who plays tricks on people?‚Äù

There was another laugh, which seemed to come from high up in the branches; then sounds like the chattering of squirrels, followed by low whistles and bird calls. They examined 150 the branches of the trees around them, but there was nothing in sight.

‚ÄúOh, go along!‚Äù exclaimed Alfred angrily. ‚ÄúOnly cowards hide behind trees. Brave men show themselves.‚Äù

Silence greeted this sally, also, and they trudged on through the forest without any further effort to see the annoyer. Several times acorn shells whizzed past their heads, and once Jimmie made a running jump, thinking he saw some one behind a tree, but returned crestfallen. A surprise was in store for them, however. They had been walking for some time when the trail, which hitherto had run straight through the middle of the wood, gave a sudden and unexpected turn, to avoid a depression in the land, overgrown with vines and small trees, and now dry from the drought.

They paused a moment on the curve of the path to look across at the graceful little hollow which seemed to be the meeting place of slender young pine trees and silver birches gleaming white among the dark green branches.

‚ÄúHow like people they look,‚Äù Bab whispered. She never knew just why she did so. ‚ÄúLike girls in white dresses at a party.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd the pine trees are the men,‚Äù whispered Jimmie. ‚ÄúLook,‚Äù he said excitedly, under his breath, ‚Äúthere‚Äôs a man! Perhaps it‚Äôs the‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù 151

He stopped short and his voice died away in amazement. Barbara said ‚ÄúSh-h-h!‚Äù and the others paused in wonder. Just emerging from the hollow on the other side, was the figure of a man. ‚ÄúBy Jove!‚Äù said Jimmie, ‚Äúnothing but a tricky foreigner, after all, and I was just beginning to like him too.‚Äù

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs more than a trickster,‚Äù Bab whispered. ‚ÄúHe‚Äôs wearing a green velveteen suit.‚Äù

Barbara began to consider. Should she tell about the knife, or should she wait? She believed that if she told it would only complicate matters and bring Zerlina, the Gypsy girl, into the muddle. Presently the jangle of a bell was heard in the distance, a pleasant musical tinkle in the midst of the green stillness of the forest.

‚ÄúGood day, Adam,‚Äù said Stephen. ‚ÄúHow are you, and how is the wood business?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a shame, Adam,‚Äù replied Stephen. ‚ÄúIt must be like cutting down your own family, you 154 have lived among them for so many years. How is the hermit? Do you give him enough wood to keep him alive in the winter?‚Äù

CHAPTER XV‚ÄîTHE HERMIT

‚ÄúDo you know,‚Äù exclaimed Ruth, ‚ÄúI feel as if I were in an enchanted forest, and these strange people were witches and wizards! The robber might have been a wood-elf, and now here comes the old witch. Perhaps she will turn us into trees and animals.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, that is old Jennie, who gathers herbs 155 and sells them at all the drugstores in the towns around here,‚Äù replied Stephen, as a strange figure came into view.

The gatherer of herbs and roots was not, however, very witchlike in appearance. She was tall and erect, and walked with long strides like a grenadier. What was most remarkable about her were her wide, staring blue eyes, like patches of sky, that looked far beyond the young people who had grouped themselves at the side of the path almost timidly, waiting for her to come up. She carried with her a staff, and as she walked she poked the bushes and grasses with it as if it had been a long finger feeling for trophies. The other hand grasped the end of an apron made of an old sack, stuffed full of herbs still green, and fragrant from having been bruised as she crushed them into the bag.

‚ÄúShe is blind,‚Äù whispered Stephen, ‚Äúbut in a minute she will perceive that some one is near. She has a scent as keen as a hunting dog‚Äôs.‚Äù

A few yards away from them old Jennie paused and sniffed the air like an animal. Reaching out with her stick she felt around her. Presently the staff pointed in the direction of the boys and girls, and she came toward them as straight as a hunter after his quarry. The girls, a little frightened, started to draw back.

‚ÄúShe won‚Äôt hurt you,‚Äù whispered Stephen. 156 ‚ÄúWhy, Jennie,‚Äù he said in a louder voice, ‚Äúdon‚Äôt you know your old friend and playmate?‚Äù

A smile broke out on Jennie‚Äôs handsome face, which, in spite of her age, was as smooth and placid as a child‚Äôs.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs Master Stephen!‚Äù she cried, in a strange voice that sounded rusty from lack of use. ‚ÄúI be glad to hear you, sir. It‚Äôs a long time since we‚Äôve had a frolic in the woods. ‚ÄúWe are both well, Jennie, and you must come over to the hall and see us. We may have something nice for you, there, that will keep you warm when the snow comes.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh, you‚Äôre a good boy, Master Stephen, and I‚Äôll bid ye good day now, and good day to your friends. ‚ÄúWhat a strange old woman!‚Äù cried Stephen‚Äôs companions in one voice.

‚ÄúAnd the strangest thing about her,‚Äù replied Stephen, ‚Äúis that she has no sense of time. ‚ÄúWhere does she live?‚Äù asked Bab.

‚ÄúShe lives in a little cabin off in the forest somewhere. Her father and mother were woodcutters. She was born and brought up right here. She doesn‚Äôt know anything but herbs and roots, and night and day are the same to her. She knows every square foot of this country, and never gets lost. ‚ÄúNo wonder you love these woods, Stephen,‚Äù said Bab. ‚ÄúThere is so much to do and see in them. I wish we had something better than scrub oak around Kingsbridge.‚Äù

‚ÄúWait until you see the chief treasure of the woods, Barbara, and you‚Äôll have even more respect for them.‚Äù

‚ÄúMeaning the hermit?‚Äù asked Jimmie. 158

‚ÄúThe greatest mystery of the countryside,‚Äù replied Stephen. ‚ÄúNobody knows where he came from, nor why he has been living here all these years‚Äîit‚Äôs about fifty, they say. ‚ÄúHow does he live?‚Äù asked Alfred.

While they were talking they had approached a clearing on the side of a hill. Most of the big trees had been cut away, and only the silver birch, ‚Äúthe white ladies,‚Äù as old Adam had christened them, and the dogwood, mingled their shade over the smooth turf. The house, a log cabin with a big stone chimney at one end, commanded a view, from the back, of a long stretch of valley. The portico in front was shaded by honeysuckle vines. Here, in an old-fashioned armchair, sat the master smoking a meerschaum pipe.

The place was certainly comfortable and cosy-looking, in spite of the wooden walls and bare floors. It was spick and span and clean, kept that way by Adam‚Äôs wife, Stephen explained. ‚ÄúCome here, everybody, quick,‚Äù she called, ‚Äúand look at this miniature. As I live, it‚Äôs enough like Bab to be a picture of her, except for the old-fashioned dress and long ringlets.‚Äù

CHAPTER XVI‚ÄîA SURPRISE

When the last sandwich had been eaten, and the last crumb of cake disposed of, the picnic party leaned lazily against the moss-covered trunk of a fallen tree to discuss the events of the morning.

Jos√© was the subject of the talk. All were inclined to believe, now, that they had been deceived by the strong resemblance between the young Spaniard and the mischievous person who had mystified them in the woods that morning. ‚ÄúIt is strange,‚Äù said Ruth. ‚ÄúHe must have the nameless charm, because there is not one of us who does not like him. As for me, I feel sorry for him. And why, I‚Äôd like to know?‚Äù

‚ÄúSo ho!‚Äù he cried. ‚ÄúWe have here five rich, young persons‚Äîrich with the money they have no right to‚Äîstolen money‚Äîstolen from me and mine. 185CHAPTER XVIII‚ÄîTHE MASQUERADE

191CHAPTER XIX‚ÄîA RECOGNITION

The masqueraders had separated for the night; Bab, however, had asked to speak with the major before he went to his room. For half an hour she was closeted with him in his library. The time had arrived to tell him everything she knew about Jos√©.

CHAPTER XX‚ÄîTHE FIRE BRIGADE

Ruth and Barbara related to Miss Sallie their adventures of the day before. She went through a dozen stages of emotion, and fairly wrung her hands over the tramps. The part about Jos√© she could not believe.

‚ÄúThe wind must be blowing from the mountains to-day,‚Äù she observed. ‚ÄúI smell burning as plainly as if it were at our gates.‚Äù

‚ÄúMy child, we are not a fire department,‚Äù exclaimed Miss Sallie, ‚Äúand if we are to save this beautiful house, how do you propose to do it?‚Äù
