‚ÄúIt may not be necessary to use the water,‚Äù Bab continued. ‚ÄúThe first thing to do is to cut off the forest fire by having a trench dug on that side of the house. Everybody will have to get to work. Come on! We must not lose time.‚Äù

Miss Sallie ran into the hall and rang a bell violently. John, the butler, came at once.

‚ÄúJohn,‚Äù she cried, speaking very rapidly, ‚Äúthe forest is on fire. Get every available person 203 on the place as fast as you can, with shovels and hoes and help the young ladies dig a trench to protect the major‚Äôs house.‚Äù

John looked dazed, sniffed the air and ran without a word. Presently a bell thundered out in the stillness. It had not been rung for many years, but the employees on the place knew what it meant, and came running from their cottages, and the work of digging a trench beyond Ten Eyck Hall was begun. Each moment the air was growing more dense and a darkness was settling down which was lit up, toward the west, by a lurid glow. The heat was intense and fine ashes filled the toilers‚Äô throats and nostrils. Birds, blinded by the smoke dashed past, almost hitting the workers‚Äô faces. People came running from the burning forest, the old Gypsy woman and her granddaughter and other women from the Gypsy band. The men were bringing the wagons around by the road; old Adam and his wife, driving their wood cart and frantically beating the worn-out horse; and finally, the hermit, with his white locks flying. Ten Eyck Hall would seem to have been the refuge of all these terrified dwellers in the forest. They regarded it with pride and love. Even the Gypsies had sought its protection, and the gray, rambling old place appeared to stretch out its arms to them. Blind Jennie strode up and down the 204 lawn, wildly waving her stick, while old Adam called to Miss Sallie:

‚ÄúWhere is the master? Where are the young masters?‚Äù

And where were the old master and the young ones? If ever they were needed, it was now!

In the meantime, the girls, leaving Miss Sallie to direct the digging of the trench, had run to the house.

‚ÄúI think, Ruth,‚Äù called Bab, ‚Äúwe had better collect all the buckets and pails we can find.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù replied Ruth, ‚Äúand the hose should be attached to the reserve well. John is attending to that. Mollie and Grace, run and get whatever blankets there are in the bed rooms, and close the windows all over the house.‚Äù

While John was attaching the hose to the faucet of the reserve well, Ruth and Bab invaded the enormous kitchen of the hall. The servants had fled. Only Mary and John could be depended upon. The pumping engine had been started and the tank was rapidly filling.

‚ÄúO Ruth,‚Äù exclaimed Bab, ‚Äúhow careless of us to have forgotten the cars! The garage is nearest to the forest and the automobiles should be run out right off. We may need them if things get very bad.‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course,‚Äù replied Ruth. ‚ÄúWhere is the 205 chauffeur? Did you ever know any of these people to be on hand when they were needed?‚Äù

Dashing to the garage, they cranked up the two machines and ran them out onto the lawn in an open space. Jos√©‚Äôs motor cycle came next.

‚ÄúThe fire has come,‚Äù cried Grace and Mollie running up with their arms full of blankets. They could hear the roaring, crackling sound as the flames licked their way through the dry underbrush.

‚ÄúWhere is Miss Sallie?‚Äù demanded Ruth. ‚ÄúShe will faint in this terrible atmosphere.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere she is,‚Äù answered Grace; ‚Äúshe is overseeing the trench-digging. I think she has ordered them to make it broader.‚Äù

Miss Sallie, her lavender skirts caught up over her arm, was standing near the men, giving her orders as calmly as if she were in her own drawing room.

The line of forest about a quarter of a mile distant began to glow red. The girls clutched each other.

‚ÄúThere it is!‚Äù they cried. ‚ÄúAnd now to save the major‚Äôs house!‚Äù

Bab organized a bucket brigade with Mollie, Grace and the Gypsy women. John was ordered to manipulate the hose, while Bab and Ruth carried wet blankets over to the garage, the building nearest the line of fire. Then a cry 206 went up from the men who were digging the trench. The flames, which had been steadily devouring the dried grass of the meadow dividing the garden from the wood, had reached the trench. A sudden gust of wind carried them over. Instantly a group of bushes caught fire; and, like an angry animal seeking its prey, a long, forked tongue licked the ground hungrily for a moment, paused at the gravel walk, followed its edge, eating up the short, dry grass in its path, and made for the garage. All this happened in much quicker time than it takes to tell it‚Äîtoo quickly, in fact for any precaution.

CHAPTER XXI‚ÄîFIGHTING THE FLAMES

Never had ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls‚Äù displayed greater courage than at this critical moment. It was the time for quick action and quicker thought. The men who were digging the trench could not leave their work. They saw that, unless the trench were dug wider, it would be necessary to fight the flames back, and they were digging like mad to keep the fire from leaping the ditch again.

It was Mollie who saved them from a terrible 207 explosion by remembering the house where the gasoline was stored just behind the garage, and John and Adam rolled the tank to a distance temporarily safe at least.

Bab had found a ladder somewhere. Placing it against the garage she had scaled it like a monkey, carrying under one arm a wet blanket the weight of which she was too excited to notice. She never quite knew how she shinned up the roof, but presently she found herself astride the pinnacle. Zerlina had followed close behind, with more blankets and together the two girls spread them over the smoking shingles. When the roof was covered, they let themselves down and began dashing water on the smouldering walls. The bucket brigade was working well under the direction of Ruth, and the garage was saved.

Then a line of clipped bushes running from the garden to the forest, suddenly burst into flames. A cry went up from the workers at this terrifying spectacle. To the girls, it seemed like a gigantic boa constrictor racing toward them, and, for a moment, they turned cold with fear.

‚ÄúAll hands must help here!‚Äù cried Bab, taking command, as she naturally did in times of danger. ‚ÄúZerlina, tell the men to come from the trench with their shovels. Bring pails of 208 water, all of you,‚Äù she called to the Gypsies, ‚Äúand the rest of the wet blankets.‚Äù

There was a rush and a scramble. They tried to beat down the angry little flames, dashed water on to them, choked them with wet blankets, trampled on them, and finally fell back, stifled and blinded with smoke and ashes, only to find the gasoline house a burning mass. It had gone up like a tinder box in an instant, and was reduced to ruins.

‚ÄúIf we have any more gusts of wind like that last, Bab, we are lost!‚Äù cried Ruth, sobbing a little under her breath. ‚ÄúBut, of course, if the worst happens, we can always take the automobiles. They can run faster than the flames.‚Äù

Back of the garage they could see another line of flames advancing like a regiment of cavalry.

‚ÄúGreat heavens!‚Äù cried Grace. ‚ÄúWhat shall we do now?‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt despair, yet,‚Äù answered Bab. ‚ÄúThose dividing hedges are very dry, but the flames don‚Äôt spread from them so quickly; and, besides, I believe the trench will stop them.‚Äù

‚ÄúO Bab,‚Äù exclaimed Ruth, ‚Äúdo you think there will ever be an end to this? We are too tired to dig trenches, and the water is getting alarmingly low.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut there are two more cisterns,‚Äù replied the undaunted Bab. 209

Just then the wind, which, up to this time, except for a few brief gusts, had been merely a breeze, gathered new strength. Sparks began to fly from the burning underbrush in the wood. It had been a ground fire, owing to the long drought, and the trees still waved their green branches over the ruins at their feet.

Ruth seized Bab‚Äôs hand convulsively.

‚ÄúYoung ladies!‚Äù called a voice behind them. Turning, they confronted the hermit. ‚ÄúI am a very old man, but, if you will permit me, I will make a suggestion. The trench will stop the fire, but it cannot keep back the sparks and I see a wind has come up that is most dangerous.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, thank you,‚Äù cried the two girls, seeing the wisdom of his suggestion immediately.

Miss Sallie, a tragic spectacle, came from around the house; her white hair tumbling down her back, her face gray with ashes and her lavender garments torn and wet.

‚ÄúGirls,‚Äù she murmured, her voice trembling, from fatigue and excitement, ‚Äúwe have done all we could do for the major. I think we had better give it up and go while we can get away.‚Äù

‚ÄúLet us have one more chance. Aunt Sallie, dearest,‚Äù begged Ruth, ‚Äúand if that fails there will still be time to get away in the motor car.‚Äù 210

‚ÄúWhat are you going to do now, child?‚Äù asked the poor woman distractedly.

‚ÄúYou go and sit down in one of the long chairs on the piazza and rest,‚Äù replied her niece, patting her hand tenderly, ‚Äúand leave everything to us.‚Äù

The girls could hear the throbbing of the pumping engine somewhere below, as they dashed up the steps. John had connected all the cisterns and the machinery was working in good order. The candles and lanterns they carried hardly made an impression in the blackness of the great empty garret, but an exclamation from John called attention to the fact that the sliding partition was down.

‚ÄúI never knew it to happen before,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúexcept once when I was too small to understand.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow are we going to manage?‚Äù asked Grace, looking overhead.

‚ÄúThrough the scuttle to the roof,‚Äù replied Barbara, pointing to a ladder leading to a trapdoor.

John climbed up first, opening the scuttle, and everybody lent a hand in lifting out the hose he had brought along. Barbara and Zerlina followed to the roof, which was steep and much broken by pinnacles and turrets; yet in contrast with the attic it was quite light outside, 211 and the girls could see perfectly where to step without slipping.

Only two people were needed, it was decided. Bab would not hear of Ruth‚Äôs coming, on account of the latter‚Äôs horror of high places. It was certain that Mollie and Grace were not agile enough for the experiment, and Bab and Zerlina had already proved what they could do when they scaled the garage roof.

The three girls left behind climbed onto a balcony just outside one of the attic windows and watched, with tremulous interest, what was happening on the roof.

Thus Zerlina and Barbara, with old John, were left alone on top of Ten Eyck Hall. They had a wonderful view of the smoking forest, the tops of whose trees were waving in the steadily rising wind. The trench had, indeed, stopped the course of the flames which had run along the meadow hedges, and there were no more lines of fire to be seen; but there was a bright glow toward the back and a sound of crackling wood. Then came a burst of flames and the onlooker saw that the stable was burning. A spark lit on Bab‚Äôs wrist; another touched her on the cheek, and presently a gust of wind brought dozens of them twinkling like shooting stars at night. They fell on the shingled roof, smouldered for a moment and went out. Others 212 followed. It could be only a matter of a little while, thought Bab, before the hall would be in flames if they were not prompt with the water.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs all right, Miss,‚Äù called John‚Äôs voice from behind the tank on the part of the roof over the attic. There was a gurgling noise and a swift jet of water burst from the nozzle of the hose.

With Zerlina‚Äôs assistance, Bab began watering the roof. But the tallest peak was beyond reach of the hose. There the sparks were smouldering into life and Bab distinctly saw a a little puff of flame lick out and then go back again like a cunning animal biding its time.

Bab ran over to the tank.

‚ÄúJohn,‚Äù she called, ‚Äúget a ladder and a pail.‚Äù

Together they unhooked the ladder attached to the tank and dragged it over to the high center peak of the roof. There was a pail, also, which they filled with water. While the old man held the ladder Bab climbed up, taking the pail from Zerlina. Several times the brave girl dashed water over the smoking shingles until every spark was dead. Then, standing on one foot, on the top rung of the ladder, Bab braced herself with a lightning rod running up the side of the turret, and leaned over to see if all were well on its other section. Below her she could see the girls on the balcony peering up at her with frightened eyes. Lifting herself entirely off the ladder, for an instant, Bab glanced around the turret. In slipping back, her foot missed the rung. The shock made her lose her grip on the lightning rod, and like a flash she slid down the steepest part of the roof now slippery from its recent wetting. There was nothing to hold to, nothing to cling to, and she closed her eyes from the horror that was before her.

It is said that a great many things pass through one‚Äôs mind at such brief, tense moments as these, when death is almost certain.

The wind had risen to a gale and the skies were overhung with a black canopy of clouds kindled by occasional flashes of lightning. There was a low grumbling sound of thunder. CHAPTER XXIV‚ÄîGOOD-BYE TO TEN EYCK HALL

Four days had passed since the exciting happenings of that eventful day that had begun with the disappearance of Jos√©, and had ended with his discovery.

‚ÄúI have much to be thankful for,‚Äù said the 230 major to Miss Sallie, who was reclining in a steamer chair on the piazza. She had not left her bed until the afternoon of the third day, and was still a little shaky and nervous.

‚ÄúI can‚Äôt think what they are, John,‚Äù she replied severely. ‚ÄúYou have had nothing but misfortunes since we came to stay under your roof. I hope they may end when we leave.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe first one,‚Äù said the major, smiling good-humoredly, ‚Äúis that I have had the privilege of knowing how splendid American women can be in time of danger. I always admired the women of my country, but never so much as now,‚Äù he added, looking fondly at his old friend.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù assented Miss Sallie proudly, ‚Äúmy girls are about as fine as any to be found in the world, I think. They are wholesome, sensible, and never cowardly. Undoubtedly they saved Ten Eyck Hall for you, Major, by their combined efforts, and by Bab‚Äôs bravery in watering the roof when the sparks began to fly.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou were just as wonderful as the girls, Sallie, my dear. They tell me you superintended the digging of the trench and managed your men with the coolness of a general; and that when the fire leaped over the trench you were there with the bucket brigade to put it out. The girls were no whit less courageous in your day than they are now, Sallie.‚Äù 231

‚ÄúAnd what is the second blessing you have to be thankful for, John?‚Äù interrupted Miss Sallie.

‚ÄúThat Jos√© is the boy I took him to be‚Äîa good, honest, noble fellow.‚Äù

‚ÄúI must say I liked him from the first moment I set eyes upon him,‚Äù said Miss Stuart.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù continued the major; ‚Äúhis father might well be proud of him. He deserves the highest commendation for his forbearance and unselfishness in regard to that brother of his.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow is the brother, by the way?‚Äù asked Miss Sallie.

‚ÄúYou know he was taken to the hospital the day after he was brought here; well, the boys went over in the car yesterday. Antonio is much better. His sister is tending him. He is very repentant, she says, and has consented to go to school and turn over a new leaf. In fact, I myself have had a long talk with him. I can see that there is great good in the boy. It has simply been perverted by evil associations.‚Äù

‚ÄúAh, Major,‚Äù exclaimed his old friend, smiling indulgently as she tapped his arm with her fan, ‚Äúyou are truly the most optimistic soul in the world. I hope all your golden dreams about this wretched boy‚Äôs future will come true. But what about his sister!‚Äù

‚ÄúJos√© is anxious for her to go to a school in America. He believes she could not endure the 232 restraint of a European school after her free, open-air life. She is only too anxious. She wants to cultivate her voice, and the old grandmother appears really relieved at the turn affairs have taken. She was willing to concede anything to keep the grandson out of jail.‚Äù

‚ÄúThen my Ruth will not be able to gratify her whim to educate the Gypsy girl,‚Äù pursued Miss Sallie.

‚ÄúNot exactly,‚Äù replied the major. ‚ÄúJos√©‚Äôs father is very well-to-do, as the world goes, but Ruth is to take charge of Zerlina‚Äôs education and look after her generally. She has asked Jos√© to allow her that privilege, as she put it.‚Äù

Just then the girls came around the corner of the piazza, after a stroll in the garden.

‚ÄúHow fresh and delicious the air is since the rain!‚Äù exclaimed Barbara. ‚ÄúThere is still a faint smell of burning. Do you think all the trees in the forest will die, Major?‚Äù

‚ÄúOld Adam says they will not,‚Äù answered the major. ‚ÄúA three months‚Äô unbroken drought will dry up almost anything but trees. Now, while the underbrush and dried fern burned like tinder, the fire hardly touched the trees. It was those dead bramble hedges dividing the fields and the dried meadow grass that did the most damage, because the sparks from them ignited the garage and the roof of the stable.‚Äù 233

‚ÄúI am glad papa and Mrs. Thurston were not uneasy about us,‚Äù observed Ruth. ‚ÄúIf they had read the papers before you telegraphed, Major, they would have been frantic, I suppose.‚Äù

‚ÄúMake way for the Duke of Granada,‚Äù called Jimmie‚Äôs cheerful voice from the hall, and presently he appeared, pushing Jos√©, done up in bandages and lying flat on his back, on a rolling cot used by some invalid of the Ten Eyck family long since dead and gone.

‚ÄúJos√©, my boy,‚Äù exclaimed the major, going to the foot of the cot to ease it as it passed over the door sill, ‚Äúdo you think this is safe?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe doctor says it will not hurt him,‚Äù replied Jimmie. ‚ÄúHe needs company, but we won‚Äôt let him stay long.‚Äù

Jos√© smiled up at the faces leaning over him.

‚ÄúYou have all been so good to me,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI want to thank you for your kindness and for believing in me when my character looked black enough to have condemned me without any more proof. And I want to thank you for my brother, too, and my poor little sister.‚Äù

His eyes filled with tears.

‚ÄúThere, there,‚Äù cried the major, pressing the boy‚Äôs hand. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a little enough we have done, I‚Äôm sure. I only wish we could have saved you from your tumble,‚Äù he added, gazing sadly toward the right wing of Ten Eyck Hall. 234

‚ÄúAnd is it really true that our friends are going to leave us this afternoon?‚Äù asked Jos√©.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù answered the major; ‚Äúall our girls and boys are going. We shall be lonesome enough when they are gone.‚Äù

There was the sound of a motor horn down the avenue.

‚ÄúAh, here comes Stephen at last. I was afraid he would be late,‚Äù said Major Ten Eyck, as his automobile pulled up at the door and Stephen, Martin and Alfred jumped out.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve got them, uncle,‚Äù cried Stephen. ‚ÄúThey arrived this morning.‚Äù And he handed his uncle a registered package carefully done up and sealed with red sealing wax.

The major took the box and disappeared into the house while the boys exchanged significant looks.

‚ÄúStephen,‚Äù said Bab, as they strolled down to the end of the-piazza while the others were examining the morning papers and reading their mail, ‚Äúdid you ever ask Jos√© where he was the morning we went to see the hermit!‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, yes,‚Äù replied her friend; ‚Äúor, rather, he told me without being asked. He was to meet his brother by appointment at the haunted pool. I suppose he was there too soon, because Antonio chose to inflict us with his antics before he went to see Jos√©, who heard a great deal of the 235 nonsense, so he said, and there was a quarrel afterwards, a very bitter one, and Jos√© threatened to give Antonio over to the authorities unless he consented to give up his lawless life. Zerlina was hovering around later, and heard the pistol shots after the fight with the tramps. She thought, of course, it was a duel between her two brothers. That is why she paid you the mysterious visit and tried to read the note.‚Äù

‚ÄúHow does Antonio strike you?‚Äù asked Bab.

‚ÄúJust as a mischievous boy might. I think he will outgrow his vicious tendencies now that he has been taken hold of. For one thing he no longer hates poor old Jos√©. I told him, plainly, what a fine fellow his brother was, and that it was only on Jos√©‚Äôs account we were not going to have him arrested. He seemed to be a good deal impressed, I think.‚Äù

‚ÄúA note for you, Miss,‚Äù said John, handing Bab a three-cornered missive on a tray.

‚ÄúWill Miss Barbara Thurston grant one last interview to an old admirer?‚Äù the note ran.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs from your great-uncle,‚Äù exclaimed Bab, giving Stephen the note to read.

Stephen smiled as his eye took in the crabbed, old-fashioned handwriting.

‚ÄúThe poor old fellow can‚Äôt quite get the proper focus as to who you really are,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúYou appear to represent two Barbaras 236 to him. But you will go over for a few minutes, won‚Äôt you, Bab? I doubt if Uncle Stephen will last much longer, and seeing you may be a great comfort to him.‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course I will,‚Äù Bab replied. ‚ÄúIf seeing me can bring a ray of pleasure into his life, I am glad enough to be able to do it. I should like to take him a few flowers. I know he loves them. Suppose we get some honeysuckle and late roses out of the garden before we go.‚Äù

Together they strolled toward the major‚Äôs garden, which the flames had spared, partly because it was protected by a high brick wall on three sides, and partly owing to a daily watering it had received from the gardener.

With Stephen‚Äôs penknife they clipped a bunch of dewy white roses with yellow centers, and a few sprays of honeysuckle whose fragrance was overpoweringly sweet.

The old man was watching for the young people at the window when the attendant opened the door for them. He came forward with some of the major‚Äôs grace and took Barbara‚Äôs hand in his.

‚ÄúIt was very good of you to come,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI heard you were going, and I wanted to say a last good-bye. I feel happier than I have felt in many years. You have forgiven me, have you not, little Barbara?‚Äù he went on, his mind 237 confusing her again with that other Barbara whose tragic death had bereft him of his reason. ‚ÄúAnd you have brought me the roses, too?‚Äù

She nodded her head.

‚ÄúDid they come from the bush near the arbor?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù she replied, wondering a little.

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you remember that it was our bush, the one we chose when you were here on a visit? Our white rose bush, Barbara. That you should not have forgotten, after all these years!‚Äù Then his memory came back. ‚ÄúBut what am I saying?‚Äù he exclaimed. ‚ÄúMy mind often gets confused. It was the likeness, I suppose. I want you to see this portrait of your grand-aunt.‚Äù

He went over to a desk near the window and drew from one of its drawers an old daguerreotype.

‚ÄúIt is very, very like,‚Äù he murmured, as he handed it to Barbara.

It was, indeed, even more like the present Bab than the miniature which the hermit had treasured during his years of solitude.

‚ÄúI want you to keep this picture, Barbara,‚Äù said Stephen‚Äôs uncle. ‚ÄúI have another one, and it will be a pleasure to me, at the last, to know that it belongs to another Barbara Thurston. 238 This ring must also be yours.‚Äù He drew from the desk a little black velvet case. ‚ÄúIt was a ring I gave to her after we were engaged. Will you wear it for me!‚Äù

Barbara opened the case and slipped the ring on her finger. It was a very old ring of beaten silver with a sapphire setting.

‚ÄúThank you,‚Äù she said and gave him her hand.

‚ÄúGood-bye, little Barbara!‚Äù cried the old man. ‚ÄúYou have brought peace to me at last. You and my dear friend, Richard. I have changed a great deal, you see,‚Äù he was lapsing back into the old mania, ‚Äúbut you are as young and pretty as ever, Barbara.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt is time to go,‚Äù whispered Stephen, hurriedly. The attendant had already opened the door for them and they slipped out together.

‚ÄúThe hermit has promised to come and see him every day,‚Äù said Stephen, as they hastened through the passage. ‚ÄúIndeed, Uncle John has invited the hermit to live at Ten Eyck Hall for the rest of his days, and he has all but consented. He is a wonderful old man, I think, and whether he swam off and left ‚Äòyou‚Äô or not, he has atoned for it after all these years.‚Äù

‚ÄúStephen,‚Äù replied Barbara, ‚ÄúI shall never believe that he did that, no matter if he were to tell me so himself.‚Äù 239

They reached the piazza just in time to hear Miss Sallie saying:

‚ÄúGirls, I think we had better go up and get ready for the trip, before luncheon is announced. We want to start promptly, this time, even if we shall have such an excellent guard of young men. Jos√©, I am sorry you are not well enough to come in to our last meal,‚Äù she added, turning to the sick boy and taking his hand. ‚ÄúBut we shall run up and say good-bye to you before we leave, and if ever you go as far west as Chicago, I want you to come and see us. Perhaps Ruth and I shall see you and your father this autumn when we are in Europe.‚Äù

‚ÄúIndeed, I hope you will come to Madrid and visit at my home,‚Äù cried Jos√©. ‚ÄúWill you not arrange it?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat would be delightful‚Äù said Miss Sallie, ‚Äúbut we shall be over only for six weeks. We must return in time for Ruth‚Äôs school, you know.‚Äù

The last luncheon at Ten Eyck Hall was a very gay one. The dangers of the previous week were over and the mysteries cleared away.

The major fairly beamed on his guests across the hospitable board.

‚ÄúIt must have been Miss Sallie‚Äôs fault,‚Äù thought Mollie, watching his handsome face with a secret admiration. ‚ÄúHe is certainly the 240 dearest old man alive. I wonder if she isn‚Äôt sorry now?‚Äù

And as if in answer to her unspoken question, she heard Miss Sallie saying:

‚ÄúJohn, I hope this is not the last visit you will let us make to Ten Eyck Hall. In spite of its fires and tramps I should like to come again.‚Äù

‚ÄúI should be the happiest man in the world if you only would,‚Äù he answered. ‚ÄúI am greatly relieved that you haven‚Äôt got an everlasting prejudice against it.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen I settle down for the winter,‚Äù Jimmie Butler was heard to remark above the hum of conversation, ‚ÄúI mean to take up a certain study and not leave off studying it until I have graduated with diploma and honors.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat is it, Jimmie?‚Äù demanded the others.

‚ÄúPrize fighting,‚Äù he replied. ‚ÄúI intend to learn wrestling and boxing, likewise just plain hair-pulling and scratching. Prize fighting in all its varieties for me before another year rolls round.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou will have to go into training, then, Jim,‚Äù exclaimed Alfred. ‚ÄúYou will not be permitted to eat anything you like and not too much of anything else.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo more hot bread for you, Jimmie,‚Äù continued Stephen. ‚ÄúNo more waffles and Johnnie-cakes. You will have to punch the bag 241 mornings, when you would rather be sleeping, and give up theatres in the evenings for early bedtime. It‚Äôs a fearful life, my boy.‚Äù

‚ÄúBe that as it may,‚Äù persisted Jimmie, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going to learn how to deal a blow that will give a man a black eye the first time, and if ever I get hold of that wiry individual who gave me these in the woods, yonder,‚Äù he pointed to his red nose and discolored eye, ‚Äúhe‚Äôll get such a ‚Äòlicking‚Äô as he‚Äôll remember to his last hour. Even Stephen‚Äôs giant won‚Äôt be a match for me.‚Äù

There was joyous laughter at this, followed by remarks from Martin and Alfred of a rather sarcastic character, such as ‚ÄúGive it to him, Jimmie! Give him a bump in the ribs!‚Äù

‚ÄúI am going to have the woods patrolled, hereafter, in the summer time,‚Äù observed the major, ‚Äúand all dangerous characters will be excluded. The next time we have a house party there will be no tramps to threaten my guests.‚Äù

‚ÄúBy the way,‚Äù said Stephen, ‚Äúthe giant tramp is in the hospital now. He was drunk when the fire started, and fell asleep. He was badly burned and almost suffocated, but his poor, long-suffering wife managed to save him somehow. The other two had left him to die.‚Äù

‚ÄúWill you have him arrested when he gets well, Major?‚Äù asked Ruth.

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù replied the major, somewhat confused. 242 ‚ÄúI suppose I should, but he tells me he was despoiled of his living by a dishonest master, and I have concluded to make it up to him for being richer than he is by giving him something to do. We have several farms back in the country and I have put him in charge of the smallest one. It seems that farming is the very thing he wants to do more than anything else in life. He will have to travel a good distance before he can get anything to drink, and his wife is the happiest woman over the prospect you ever saw.‚Äù

‚ÄúMajor, major!‚Äù protested Miss Sallie. ‚ÄúWhat will you do next?‚Äù

‚ÄúAh, well,‚Äù exclaimed the major, ‚Äúit is good to be able to give a man a chance to earn an honest living, especially if he wants to take it. And, when this poor wretch heard about that bit of land and little cottage back yonder in the hills, he looked as if he had had a glimpse of heaven. His wife told me that he had really tried, again and again to find something to do; but indoor life was very irksome to him because he had been brought up on a farm, and working in factories and foundries had been his undoing.‚Äù

‚ÄúStephen, how do you feel about it?‚Äù asked Alfred. ‚ÄúHe was your opponent in the fight, you know.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, I don‚Äôt mind,‚Äù replied Stephen. ‚ÄúHe didn‚Äôt give me a black eye, and I am glad for 243 him to earn an honest living. Uncle‚Äôs a brick.‚Äù

When the meal was over Major Ten Eyck rose from the table, clearing his throat as if he were about to make a speech, which indeed he was.

‚ÄúI have something to say before this party breaks up, for myself and the boys. We want to express to you, how deeply grateful we feel to you, Miss Sallie and ‚ÄòThe Automobile Girls,‚Äô for what you have done for us.

‚ÄúYou have saved our old home for us, at the risk of your own precious lives, and there is nothing we can really do or say to show how much we appreciate it. The place has been in the family ever since there were any Ten Eycks to live in it. I was born here and I love it, and I hope to end my days here‚Äî‚Äî‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt speak as if you were on the brink of the grave, Major, I beg of you,‚Äù protested Miss Sallie. ‚ÄúYou are not many years older than I am, and I certainly will not allow such mournful thoughts to trouble me so soon.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou will always be young, Sallie,‚Äù replied the gallant major.

‚ÄúYou are nothing but a boy yourself, John,‚Äù replied Miss Stuart, blushing in spite of herself, while the young people exchanged stealthy smiles at these elderly compliments.

‚ÄúI was saying,‚Äù continued the major, who remained standing 244 to finish his speech, ‚Äúthat there was nothing we could do, the boys and I, to show how we feel in this matter. But when you wear these little ornaments‚Äù (here the major handed Miss Sallie and each of the girls a little jeweler‚Äôs box) ‚Äúwe hope you will remember that we are your devoted friends always. It was Stephen‚Äôs idea, and there was not much time to get them, but the jeweler undertook a rush order for us, and I hope they are all right.‚Äù

‚ÄúHurray!‚Äù cried Jimmie, rolling his napkin into a ball and tossing it into the air.

There were cries of pleasure when the boxes gave up their treasures, small gold firemen‚Äôs helmets studded with pearls and a row of rubies on the curve of the brim.

As if this were not enough, John came in with a tray of bouquets, each one different, as on a former occasion. The major had picked and arranged the flowers himself for Miss Sallie and ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls,‚Äù as a last reminder of Ten Eyck Hall, he said.

‚ÄúIt is worth while going into the firemen‚Äôs business, if one is to be so well repaid,‚Äù exclaimed Ruth.

Bab felt particularly rich in souvenirs of her visit, with a picture of a new and hitherto unknown great-aunt, a ring and a beautiful pin. 245

‚ÄúWe are all much too excited to thank you properly, Major,‚Äù she said.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want any thanks, my dear child,‚Äù replied the major. ‚ÄúI wish to avoid them.‚Äù

‚ÄúSomebody should make a speech,‚Äù cried Jimmie‚Äôs voice above the jollity. ‚ÄúI think I‚Äôll be the one.‚Äù He cleared his throat. ‚ÄúMajor John Ten Eyck,‚Äù he said bowing toward the major, ‚ÄúI know these young ladies appreciate deeply the handsome souvenirs you have bestowed upon them, but youth and inexperience have tied their tongues. However, mine is loosened and I wish to thank you a thousand times for the souvenirs which I also am carrying away from Ten Eyck Hall, namely my beautiful ruby nose and my blue enameled eyes.‚Äù

There was more laughter and more exchange of jokes and fun, when Martin who had slipped out of the room for a moment, returned with a small bundle which he handed to Jimmie.

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll give you a booby prize, Jimmie,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúsince the ladies have been awarded the first prize.‚Äù

Jimmie opened the bundle and drew forth a boxing glove which he put on immediately and chased Martin out of the room. This was the signal for the breaking up of the lunch party.

The boxes and suit cases were already piled in their accustomed place on the back of the 246 car and there was nothing for the girls to do but to pin on their hats and veils, slip on their silk dusters and go.

The servants had lined up in the hall to say good-bye. Jos√© had begged to be permitted to remain downstairs until after the visitors had gone. As the automobiles sped down the avenue, the major, standing by the sick boy‚Äôs cot, waved good-bye from the piazza.

Only Bab saw another handkerchief waving its pathetic farewell from a window in the right wing. She gave an answering wave with her own little handkerchief which she hoped the old man would not miss.

‚ÄúGood-bye to Ten Eyck Hall,‚Äù she said to herself as she looked back at the beautiful old house. ‚ÄúYou are full of tragic memories, but I love you and I would have risked much to have saved you from crumbling to a heap of ashes.‚Äù

As they passed over the bridge and came to the crossroads by the woods, they were stopped by blind Jennie, who silently presented Bab and Ruth each with a small cross she herself had carved from wood. Then to Bab she gave a beautiful bunch of yellow roses, which the hermit had begged the girl to accept with his best wishes.

247CHAPTER XXV‚ÄîCONCLUSION

In spite of the strange chain of events following so closely on each other‚Äôs heels, ‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls‚Äù had only pleasant memories of Ten Eyck Hall and its occupants.

Among their trips they counted this as one of the most interesting, but Ruth, who was ever planning future surprises, had a plan that would outdo all other visits. This was nothing less than a journey to her own home, Chicago.

This excursion, every moment of which was to throb with interest for our four girls, involved the attempt to discover a hidden treasure buried in what had once been the prairie home of an old Illinois family. These adventures, with exciting scenes on the Stock Exchange where Barbara Thurston learned of a plot to ruin her friends, and much more, all is vividly described in the next volume of this series:

‚ÄúThe Automobile Girls at Chicago; or, Winning Out Against Heavy Odds.‚Äù

THE END.
